href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_Tajikistan&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Sogdia</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="/&#594;/: \'o\' in \'body\'">&#594;</span><span title="/&#609;/: \'g\' in \'guy\'">&#609;</span><span title="\'d\' in \'dye\'">d</span><span title="/i/: \'y\' in \'happy\'">i</span><span title="/&#601;/: \'a\' in \'about\'">&#601;</span></span>/</a></span></span>) or <b>Sogdiana</b> was an ancient <a href="/wiki/Iranian_peoples" title="Iranian peoples">Iranian</a> civilization that at different times included territory located in present-day <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a> such as: <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Khujand" title="Khujand">Khujand</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panjikent" title="Panjikent">Panjikent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shahrisabz" title="Shahrisabz">Shahrisabz</a>. Sogdiana was also a province of the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a>, eighteenth in the list on the <a href="/wiki/Behistun_Inscription" title="Behistun Inscription">Behistun Inscription</a> of <a href="/wiki/Darius_the_Great" title="Darius the Great">Darius the Great</a> (i. 16). In the <a href="/wiki/Avesta" title="Avesta">Avesta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avestan_geography" title="Avestan geography">Sogdiana is listed</a> as the second best land that the supreme deity <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a> had created.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It comes second, after <a href="/wiki/Airyanem_Vaejah" title="Airyanem Vaejah">Airyanem Vaejah</a>, "homeland of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arya#Etymology" title="Arya">Aryans</a>", in the <a href="/wiki/Zoroaster" title="Zoroaster">Zoroastrian</a> book of <a href="/wiki/Vendidad" title="Vendidad">Vendidad</a>, indicating the importance of this region from ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1216_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1216-7">[7]</a></sup> Sogdiana was first conquered by <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a>, the founder of the Achaemenid Empire. The region would then be annexed by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedonian</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> in 328 BC. The region would continue to change hands under the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a>,  <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalite_Empire" title="Hephthalite Empire">Hephthalite Empire</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a>.\n</p><p>The Sogdian states, although never politically united, were centred on the main <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Samarkand" title="Timeline of Samarkand">city of Samarkand</a>. Sogdiana lay north of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, east of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">Khwarezm</a>, and southeast of <a href="/wiki/Kangju" title="Kangju">Kangju</a> between the Oxus (<a href="/wiki/Amu_Darya" title="Amu Darya">Amu Darya</a>) and the Jaxartes (<a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Syr Darya</a>), embracing the fertile valley of the <a href="/wiki/Zeravshan_River" title="Zeravshan River">Zeravshan</a> (ancient <i>Polytimetus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_britannica_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia_britannica-8">[8]</a></sup> Sogdian territory corresponds to the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samarkand_Province" title="Samarkand Province">provinces of Samarkand</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bukhara_Province" title="Bukhara Province">Bokhara</a> in modern Uzbekistan as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sughd" title="Sughd">Sughd</a> province of modern Tajikistan. During the <a href="/wiki/High_Middle_Ages" title="High Middle Ages">High Middle Ages</a>, Sogdian cities included sites stretching towards <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Issyk_Kul" title="Issyk Kul">Issyk Kul</a> such as that at the archeological site of <a href="/wiki/Suyab" title="Suyab">Suyab</a>. <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">Sogdian</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian language</a>, is no longer a spoken language, but its direct descendant, <a href="/wiki/Yaghnobi_language" title="Yaghnobi language">Yaghnobi</a>, is still spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Yaghnobi_people" title="Yaghnobi people">Yaghnobis</a> of Tajikistan. It was widely spoken in Central Asia as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> and even served as one of the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_Khaganate" title="Turkic Khaganate">Turkic Khaganate</a>\'s court languages for writing documents.\n</p><p>Sogdians also lived in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Imperial_China" title="Imperial China">Imperial China</a> and rose to special prominence in the military and government of the Chinese <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> (618&#8211;907 AD). Sogdian merchants and diplomats traveled as far west as the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. They played an important part as middlemen in the trade route of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>. While originally following the faiths of <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">Hinduism</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> and to a lesser extent, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christianity" title="Nestorian Christianity">Nestorian Christianity</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/West_Asia" title="West Asia">West Asia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Tajikistan" title="Islam in Tajikistan">gradual conversion to Islam</a> among the Sogdians and their descendants began with the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">Muslim conquest of Transoxiana</a> in the 8th century. The Sogdian conversion to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> was virtually complete by the end of the <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid Empire</a> in 999, coinciding with the decline of the Sogdian language, as it was largely supplanted by <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Name"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Name</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#History"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">History</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Prehistory"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Prehistory</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Achaemenid_period"><span class="toctext">Hellenistic period</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Central_Asia_and_the_Silk_Road"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Central Asia and the Silk Road</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#Trade_and_diplomacy_with_the_Byzantine_Empire"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">Trade and diplomacy with the Byzantine Empire</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#Sogdian_merchants,_generals,_and_statesmen_of_Imperial_China"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">Sogdian merchants, generals, and statesmen of Imperial China</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-9"><a href="#Arab_Muslim_conquest_of_Central_Asia"><span class="tocnumber">2.7</span> <span class="toctext">Arab Muslim conquest of Central Asia</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-10"><a href="#Language_and_culture"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Language and culture</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Art"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Art</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Language"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Language</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-13"><a href="#Clothing"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Clothing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-14"><a href="#Religious_beliefs"><span class="tocnumber">3.4</span> <span class="toctext">Religious beliefs</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Commerce_and_slave_trade"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Commerce and slave trade</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#Modern_historiography"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Modern historiography</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Notable_Sogdians"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Notable Sogdians</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-18"><a href="#Diaspora_areas"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Diaspora areas</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-19"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-20"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Citations"><span class="tocnumber">9.1</span> <span class="toctext">Citations</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-22"><a href="#Sources"><span class="tocnumber">9.2</span> <span class="toctext">Sources</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2951" data-file-width="2091" decoding="async" height="310" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG/220px-Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG/330px-Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG/440px-Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Afrasiab_-_details_from_The_Ambassadors%27_Painting.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Detail of a copy of the <a href="/wiki/Afrasiab_painting" title="Afrasiab painting">Ambassadors\' Painting</a> from <a href="/wiki/Afrasiyab_(Samarkand)" title="Afrasiyab (Samarkand)">Afrasiyab</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, showing men <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_camel" title="Bactrian camel">on a camel</a>, 7th century AD</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Oswald_Szemer%C3%A9nyi" title="Oswald Szemer&#233;nyi">Oswald Szemer&#233;nyi</a> devotes a thorough discussion to the etymologies of ancient ethnic words for the <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> in his work <i>Four Old Iranian Ethnic Names: Scythian &#8211; Skudra &#8211; Sogdian &#8211; Saka</i>. In it, the names provided by the Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> and the names of his title, except <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a>, as well as many other words for "Scythian," such as <a href="/wiki/Akkadian_language" title="Akkadian language">Assyrian</a> <i>A&#353;kuz</i> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> <i>Skuth&#275;s</i>, descend from *skeud-, an ancient <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European</a> root meaning "propel, shoot" (cf. English shoot).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> *skud- is the zero-grade; that is, a variant in which the -e- is not present. The restored Scythian name is *Skuda (<a href="/wiki/Archery" title="Archery">archer</a>), which among the Pontic or Royal Scythians became *Skula, in which the d has been regularly replaced by an l. According to Szemer&#233;nyi, Sogdiana (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Persian_language" title="Old Persian language">Old Persian</a>: <span lang="peo">Suguda-</span>; <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a>: <span lang="uz">Sug\'d, Sug\'diyona</span>; <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="fa">&#1587;&#1594;&#1583;</span>&#8206; <i>So&#289;d</i>; <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Tajik</a>: <span lang="tg">&#1057;&#1091;&#1171;&#1076;, &#1587;&#1594;&#1583;</span> <i>Su&#289;d</i>; <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh">&#31903;&#29305;</span> <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_Chinese" title="Mandarin Chinese">Mandarin</a> <i>s&#249;t&#232;</i>; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">&#931;&#959;&#947;&#948;&#953;&#945;&#957;&#942;</span>) was named from the Skuda form. Starting from the names of the province given in <a href="/wiki/Old_Persian" title="Old Persian">Old Persian</a> inscriptions, Sugda and Suguda, and the knowledge derived from Middle Sogdian that Old Persian -gd- applied to Sogdian was pronounced as voiced fricatives, -&#947;&#948;-, Szemer&#233;nyi arrives at *Su&#947;&#948;a as an Old Sogdian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endonym" title="Endonym">endonym</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Applying sound changes apparent in other Sogdian words and inherent in Indo-European he traces the development of *Su&#947;&#948;a from Skuda, "archer," as follows: Skuda &gt; *Sukuda by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaptyxis" title="Anaptyxis">anaptyxis</a> &gt; *Suku&#948;a &gt; *Suk&#948;a (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncope_(phonetics)" title="Syncope (phonetics)">syncope</a>) &gt; *Su&#947;&#948;a (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assimilation_(linguistics)" title="Assimilation (linguistics)">assimilation</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkestan" title="Turkestan">Turkestan</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Central_Asia" title="History of Central Asia">History of Central Asia</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Uzbekistan" title="History of Uzbekistan">History of Uzbekistan</a>, and <a href="/wiki/History_of_Tajikistan" title="History of Tajikistan">History of Tajikistan</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranians" title="Indo-Iranians">Indo-Iranians</a></div>\n<p>Centuries before the conquest of Sogdiana by the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid Empire</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_Persia" title="History of Persia">Persia</a>, Sogdiana possessed a <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> urban culture that was gradually displaced by the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_migrations" title="Indo-European migrations">Indo-European migrations</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a>. This <a href="/wiki/Andronovo_culture" title="Andronovo culture">large-scale migration</a> included Eastern Iranian speaking peoples such as the Sogdians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simonin_2012_sogdiana_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-simonin_2012_sogdiana-13">[13]</a></sup> The original Bronze Age towns appear in the archaeological record beginning with the settlement at <a href="/wiki/Sarazm" title="Sarazm">Sarazm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, spanning as far back as the 4th millennium BC and then at K&#246;k Tepe, near modern-day <a href="/wiki/Bulungur" title="Bulungur">Bulungur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, from at least the 15th century BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaissi&#232;re_Encyclop&#230;dia_Iranica_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaissi&#232;re_Encyclop&#230;dia_Iranica-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Achaemenid_period">Achaemenid period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Achaemenid period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_(Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22)_(4689076272).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1994" data-file-width="4180" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_%28Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22%29_%284689076272%29.jpg/300px-Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_%28Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22%29_%284689076272%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_%28Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22%29_%284689076272%29.jpg/450px-Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_%28Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22%29_%284689076272%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_%28Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22%29_%284689076272%29.jpg/600px-Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_%28Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22%29_%284689076272%29.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sogdian_Tribute_Bearers_on_the_Apadana_Staircase_16_(Best_Viewed_Size_%22Large%22)_(4689076272).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sogdians on an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Achaemenid" title="Achaemenid">Achaemenid</a> Persian <a href="/wiki/Relief" title="Relief">relief</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Apadana" title="Apadana">Apadana</a> of <a href="/wiki/Persepolis" title="Persepolis">Persepolis</a>, offering tributary gifts to the Persian king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>, 5th century BC</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:112px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="532" decoding="async" height="194" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg/110px-Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg/165px-Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg/220px-Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg 2x" width="110"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artaxerxes_III_Sogdian_soldier.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sogdian soldier circa 338 BCE, tomb of <a href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_III" title="Artaxerxes III">Artaxerxes III</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Achaemenid ruler <a href="/wiki/Cyrus_the_Great" title="Cyrus the Great">Cyrus the Great</a> conquered Sogdiana while <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wars_of_Cyrus_the_Great" title="Wars of Cyrus the Great">campaigning in Central Asia</a> in 546&#8211;539 BC,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> a fact mentioned by the ancient Greek historian <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/Histories_(Herodotus)" title="Histories (Herodotus)">Histories</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simonin_2012_sogdiana_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-simonin_2012_sogdiana-13">[13]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a> introduced the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic writing system</a> and <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_coinage" title="Achaemenid coinage">coin currency</a> to <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, in addition to incorporating Sogdians into his <a href="/wiki/Standing_army" title="Standing army">standing army</a> as regular soldiers and cavalrymen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baumer_2012_pp202-203_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-baumer_2012_pp202-203-16">[16]</a></sup> A contingent of Sogdian soldiers fought in the main army of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> during his ultimately failed <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">invasion of Greece</a> in 480 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1216_7-1"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1216-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p3_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p3-17">[17]</a></sup> A Persian inscription from <a href="/wiki/Susa" title="Susa">Susa</a> claims that the palace there was adorned with <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">carnelian</a> originating from Sogdiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1216_7-2"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1216-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Given the absence of any named <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satraps</a> (i.e. Achaemenid provincial governors) for Sogdiana in historical records, modern scholarship has concluded that Sogdiana was governed from the satrapy of nearby <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The satraps were often relatives of the ruling Persian kings, especially sons who were not designated as the <a href="/wiki/Heir_apparent" title="Heir apparent">heir apparent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-simonin_2012_sogdiana_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-simonin_2012_sogdiana-13">[13]</a></sup> Sogdiana likely remained under Persian control until roughly 400 BC, during the reign of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artaxerxes_II" title="Artaxerxes II">Artaxerxes II</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baumer_2012_p207_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-baumer_2012_p207-19">[19]</a></sup> Rebellious states of the Persian Empire took advantage of the weak Artaxerxes II, and some, such as <a href="/wiki/Twenty-eighth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Twenty-eighth Dynasty of Egypt">Egypt</a>, were able to regain their independence. Persia\'s massive loss of Central Asian territory is widely attributed to the ruler\'s lack of control. However, unlike Egypt, which was quickly recaptured by the Persian Empire, Sogdiana remained independent until it was conquered by <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>. When the latter <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">invaded the Persian Empire</a>, Pharasmanes, an already independent king of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezm" title="Khwarezm">Khwarezm</a>, allied with the Macedonians and sent troops to Alexander in 329 BC for his war against the <a href="/wiki/Scythia" title="Scythia">Scythians</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a> region (even though this anticipated campaign never materialized).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baumer_2012_p207_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-baumer_2012_p207-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Achaemenid period (550&#8211;330 BC), the Sogdians lived as a <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomadic</a> people much like the neighboring <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhi</a>, who <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_language" title="Bactrian language">spoke Bactrian</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian language</a> closely related to Sogdian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> and were already engaging in overland trade. Some of them had also gradually settled the land to engage in agriculture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2010_p67_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2010_p67-21">[21]</a></sup> Similar to how the Yuezhi offered tributary gifts of <a href="/wiki/Jade" title="Jade">jade</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_China" title="Emperor of China">emperors of China</a>, the Sogdians are recorded in Persian records as submitting precious gifts of <a href="/wiki/Lapis_lazuli" title="Lapis lazuli">lapis lazuli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carnelian" title="Carnelian">carnelian</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Darius_I" title="Darius I">Darius I</a>, the Persian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_kings" title="King of kings">king of kings</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2010_p67_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2010_p67-21">[21]</a></sup> Although the Sogdians were at times independent and living outside the boundaries of large empires, they never formed a great empire of their own like the Yuezhi, who established the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a> (30&#8211;375 AD) of Central and <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2010_p67_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2010_p67-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hellenistic_period">Hellenistic period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Hellenistic period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_Alexander_the_Great" title="Wars of Alexander the Great">Wars of Alexander the Great</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chronology_of_the_expedition_of_Alexander_the_Great_into_Asia" title="Chronology of the expedition of Alexander the Great into Asia">Chronology of the expedition of Alexander the Great into Asia</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic civilization</a></div>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:122px;max-width:122px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:UrumqiWarrior.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1000" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/BactrianZoroastrian.jpg/400px-BactrianZoroastrian.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: The <a href="/wiki/Sampul_tapestry" title="Sampul tapestry">Sampul tapestry</a>, a woolen wall hanging from <a href="/wiki/Lop_County" title="Lop County">Lop County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, showing a possibly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Greek soldier</a> from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian kingdom">Greco-Bactrian kingdom</a> (250&#8211;125 BC), with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" title="Blue eyes">blue eyes</a>, wielding a spear, and wearing what appears to be a <i><a href="/wiki/Diadem" title="Diadem">diadem</a></i> headband; depicted above him is a <a href="/wiki/Centaur" title="Centaur">centaur</a>, from <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>, a common <a href="/wiki/Motif_(visual_arts)" title="Motif (visual arts)">motif</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hellenistic_art" title="Hellenistic art">Hellenistic art</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><br/> <b>Right image</b>: painted clay and <a href="/wiki/Alabaster" title="Alabaster">alabaster</a> head of a <a href="/wiki/Mobad" title="Mobad">Zoroastrian priest</a> wearing a distinctive <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactrian</a>-style headdress, <a href="/wiki/Takhti-Sangin" title="Takhti-Sangin">Takhti-Sangin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, 3rd&#8211;2nd century BC</div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:428px;max-width:428px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="418" data-file-width="845" decoding="async" height="104" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg/210px-DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg/315px-DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg/420px-DiodotusGoldCoin.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="757" data-file-width="1516" decoding="async" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg/210px-Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg/315px-Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg/420px-Sogdian_barbaric_copy_of_a_coin_of_Euthydemus.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: a gold coin of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diodotus_of_Bactria" title="Diodotus of Bactria">Diodotus</a>, c. 250 BC<br/><b>Right image</b>: a <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">barbaric</a> copy of a coin of <a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_I" title="Euthydemus I">Euthydemus I</a>, from the region of Sogdiana; the legend on <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">the reverse</a> is in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aramaic" title="Aramaic">Aramaic</a> script.</div></div></div>\n<p>A now independent and warlike Sogdiana, led at first by <a href="/wiki/Bessus" title="Bessus">Bessus</a>, the Achaemenid <a href="/wiki/Satrap" title="Satrap">satrap</a> of <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a> and claimant to the throne after assassinating <a href="/wiki/Darius_III" title="Darius III">Darius III</a> in his flight from the <a href="/wiki/Macedonia_(ancient_kingdom)" title="Macedonia (ancient kingdom)">Macedonian Greek</a> army,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> formed a border region insulating the Achaemenid Persians from the nomadic <a href="/wiki/Scythians" title="Scythians">Scythians</a> to the north and east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_Rock" title="Sogdian Rock">Sogdian Rock</a> or Rock of Ariamazes, a fortress in Sogdiana, was captured in 327 BC by the forces of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a>, the <i><a href="/wiki/Basileus" title="Basileus">basileus</a></i> of Macedonian Greece and conqueror of the Persian Achaemenid Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oxyartes" title="Oxyartes">Oxyartes</a>, a Sogdian nobleman of Bactria, had hoped to keep his daughter <a href="/wiki/Roxana" title="Roxana">Roxana</a> safe at the fortress of the Sogdian Rock, yet after its fall Roxana was soon wed to Alexander as one of his several wives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahmed_2004_p61_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-ahmed_2004_p61-27">[27]</a></sup> Roxana, a Sogdian whose name <i>Roshanak</i> means "little star",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius_roxane_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-livius_roxane-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strachan_2008_p87_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-strachan_2008_p87-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> was the mother of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_IV_of_Macedon" title="Alexander IV of Macedon">Alexander IV of Macedon</a>, who inherited his late father\'s throne in 323 BC (although the empire was soon divided in the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Diadochi" title="Wars of the Diadochi">Wars of the Diadochi</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After an extended campaign putting down Sogdian resistance and founding military outposts manned by his Macedonian veterans, Alexander united Sogdiana with Bactria into one satrapy. The Sogdian nobleman and warlord <a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a> (370&#8211;328 BC), allied with Scythian tribes, led an uprising against Alexander\'s forces. This revolt was put down by Alexander and his generals <a href="/wiki/Amyntas_(son_of_Andromenes)" title="Amyntas (son of Andromenes)">Amyntas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Craterus" title="Craterus">Craterus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Coenus_(general)" title="Coenus (general)">Coenus</a>, with the aid of native Bactrian and Sogdian troops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holt_1989_pp64-65_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-holt_1989_pp64-65-32">[32]</a></sup> With the Scythian and Sogdian rebels defeated, Spitamenes was allegedly betrayed by his own wife and beheaded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> Pursuant with his own marriage to Roxana, Alexander encouraged his men to marry Sogdian women in order to discourage further revolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahmed_2004_p61_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-ahmed_2004_p61-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> This included <a href="/wiki/Apama" title="Apama">Apama</a>, daughter of the rebel Spitamenes, who wed <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a> and bore him <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_I_Soter" title="Antiochus I Soter">a son and future heir</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid throne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magill_et_al_1998_p1010_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-magill_et_al_1998_p1010-35">[35]</a></sup> According to the Roman historian <a href="/wiki/Appian" title="Appian">Appian</a>, Seleucus I named three new Hellenistic cities in Asia after her (see <i><a class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Apamea_(disambiguation)" title="Apamea (disambiguation)">Apamea</a></i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magill_et_al_1998_p1010_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-magill_et_al_1998_p1010-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The military power of the Sogdians never recovered. Subsequently, Sogdiana formed part of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hellenistic_civilization" title="Hellenistic civilization">Hellenistic</a> <a href="/wiki/Greco-Bactrian_Kingdom" title="Greco-Bactrian Kingdom">Greco-Bactrian Kingdom</a>, a breakaway state from the Seleucid Empire founded in 248 BC by <a href="/wiki/Diodotus_I" title="Diodotus I">Diodotus I</a>, for roughly a century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_I" title="Euthydemus I">Euthydemus I</a>, a former satrap of Sogdiana, seems to have held the Sogdian territory as a rival claimant to the Greco-Bactrian throne; <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_coinage" title="Ancient Greek coinage">his coins</a> were later copied locally and bore <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic inscriptions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The Greco-Bactrian king <a href="/wiki/Eucratides_I" title="Eucratides I">Eucratides I</a> may have recovered sovereignty of Sogdia temporarily. Finally the area was occupied by <a href="/wiki/Nomad" title="Nomad">nomads</a> when the Scythians and <a href="/wiki/Yuezhi" title="Yuezhi">Yuezhis</a> overran it around 145 BC. From then until about 40 BC the Yuezhi tepidly minted coins imitating and still bearing the images of the Greco-Bactrian kings Eucratides I and <a href="/wiki/Heliocles_I" title="Heliocles I">Heliocles I</a>, yet soon afterwards they began minting unique coins bearing the faces of their own rulers as a prelude to asserting themselves as a world power under the <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The American historian <a href="/wiki/Homer_H._Dubs" title="Homer H. Dubs">Homer H. Dubs</a> offered the suggestion that a lost <a href="/wiki/Roman_legion" title="Roman legion">legion</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_army" title="Roman army">Roman army</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crassus" title="Crassus">Crassus</a> that fought at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Carrhae" title="Battle of Carrhae">Carrhae</a> encountered and even fought a <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_China_before_1911" title="Military history of China before 1911">Chinese army</a> of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> in the region:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>...&#160;[In 36 BC a] <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" title="Han Dynasty">Han</a> expedition into central Asia, west of the <a href="/wiki/Syr_Darya" title="Syr Darya">Jaxartes River</a>, apparently encountered and defeated a contingent of Roman legionaries. The Romans may have been the enslaved remnants of Crassus\' army, defeated by the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthians</a> and forced to fight on their eastern frontier. Sogdiana (modern <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>), east of the Oxus River, on the Polytimetus River, was apparently the most easterly penetration ever made by Roman forces in Asia. The margin of Chinese victory appears to have been their <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">crossbows</a>, whose bolts and darts seem easily to have penetrated Roman shields and armour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>However, this interpretation has been disputed by scholars such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Schuyler_V._Cammann" title="Schuyler V. Cammann">Schuyler V. Cammann</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_Asia_and_the_Silk_Road">Central Asia and the Silk Road</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Central Asia and the Silk Road">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sino-Persian_relations" AD</div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Western_groom_figure%2C_China%2C_Tang_dynasty%2C_7th_century_AD%2C_gray_pottery_with_painted_ornament_-_Matsuoka_Museum_of_Art_-_Tokyo%2C_Japan_-_DSC07248.JPG/204px-Western_groom_figure%2C_China%2C_Tang_dynasty%2C_7th_century_AD%2C_gray_pottery_with_painted_ornament_-_Matsuoka_Museum_of_Art_-_Tokyo%2C_Japan_-_DSC07248.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Western_groom_figure%2C_China%2C_Tang_dynasty%2C_7th_century_AD%2C_gray_pottery_with_painted_ornament_-_Matsuoka_Museum_of_Art_-_Tokyo%2C_Japan_-_DSC07248.JPG/272px-Western_groom_figure%2C_China%2C_Tang_dynasty%2C_7th_century_AD%2C_gray_pottery_with_painted_ornament_-_Matsuoka_Museum_of_Art_-_Tokyo%2C_Japan_-_DSC07248.JPG 2x" width="136"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: A Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Han" title="Eastern Han">Eastern Han</a> (25&#8211;220 AD) <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">ceramic statuette</a> of a Sogdian caravan leader of the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, wearing a distinctive Sogdian cap<br/> <b>Right image</b>: A grey pottery figurine of a Sogdian <a href="/wiki/Groom_(profession)" title="Groom (profession)">groom</a>, Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a>, 7th century AD</div></div></div>\n<p>Most merchants did not travel the entire <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> but would trade goods through middlemen based in oasis towns such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khotan" title="Khotan">Khotan</a> or <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang" title="Dunhuang">Dunhuang</a>. The Sogdians, however, established a trading network across the 1500 miles from Sogdiana to China. In fact, the Sogdians turned their energies to trade so thoroughly that the Saka of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Kingdom of Khotan</a> called all merchants <i>suli</i>, "Sogdian", whatever their culture or ethnicity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-wood-43">[43]</a></sup>  Unlike the empires of antiquity, the Sogdian region was not a territory confined within fixed borders, but rather a network of <a href="/wiki/City-state" title="City-state">city-states</a>, from one oasis to another, linking Sogdiana to <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="History of the Byzantine Empire">Byzantium</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_India" title="History of India">India</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indochina" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> and <a href="/wiki/History_of_China" title="History of China">China</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Sogdian contacts with China were initiated by the embassy of the Chinese explorer <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Qian" title="Zhang Qian">Zhang Qian</a> during the reign of <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Wu_of_Han" title="Emperor Wu of Han">Emperor Wu</a> (r. 141&#8211;87 BC) of the former <a href="/wiki/Han_dynasty" title="Han dynasty">Han dynasty</a>. Zhang wrote a report of his visit to the <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a> in Central Asia and named the area of Sogdiana as "<a href="/wiki/Kangju" title="Kangju">Kangju</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:361px;max-width:361px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:164px;max-width:164px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hommes_au_banquet,_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre,_Penjikent,_Tadjikistan.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3047" data-file-width="3806" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Hommes_au_banquet%2C_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre%2C_Penjikent%2C_Tadjikistan.jpg/162px-Hommes_au_banquet%2C_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre%2C_Penjikent%2C_Tadjikistan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Hommes_au_banquet%2C_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre%2C_Penjikent%2C_Tadjikistan.jpg/243px-Hommes_au_banquet%2C_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre%2C_Penjikent%2C_Tadjikistan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Hommes_au_banquet%2C_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre%2C_Penjikent%2C_Tadjikistan.jpg/324px-Hommes_au_banquet%2C_pigment_sur_pl%C3%A2tre%2C_Penjikent%2C_Tadjikistan.jpg 2x" width="162"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:193px;max-width:193px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fregio_rosso,_palazzo_di_varakhsha,_camera_11,_pareti_sud_ed_est_(parz),_VII-VIII_sec,_03.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1911" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Fregio_rosso%2C_palazzo_di_varakhsha%2C_camera_11%2C_pareti_sud_ed_est_%28parz%29%2C_VII-VIII_sec%2C_03.JPG/191px-Fregio_rosso%2C_palazzo_di_varakhsha%2C_camera_11%2C_pareti_sud_ed_est_%28parz%29%2C_VII-VIII_sec%2C_03.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Fregio_rosso%2C_palazzo_di_varakhsha%2C_camera_11%2C_pareti_sud_ed_est_%28parz%29%2C_VII-VIII_sec%2C_03.JPG/287px-Fregio_rosso%2C_palazzo_di_varakhsha%2C_camera_11%2C_pareti_sud_ed_est_%28parz%29%2C_VII-VIII_sec%2C_03.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Fregio_rosso%2C_palazzo_di_varakhsha%2C_camera_11%2C_pareti_sud_ed_est_%28parz%29%2C_VII-VIII_sec%2C_03.JPG/382px-Fregio_rosso%2C_palazzo_di_varakhsha%2C_camera_11%2C_pareti_sud_ed_est_%28parz%29%2C_VII-VIII_sec%2C_03.JPG 2x" width="191"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: Sogdian men feasting and eating at a banquet, from a wall mural of <a href="/wiki/Panjakent" title="Panjakent">Panjakent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>, 7th century AD<br/> <b>Right image</b>: Detail from another wall mural from Panjakent, 7th century AD, showing <a href="/wiki/Tiger" title="Tiger">tigers</a> attacking a man riding a <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">war elephant</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Following Zhang Qian\'s embassy and report, commercial Chinese relations with Central Asia and Sogdiana flourished,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> as many Chinese missions were sent throughout the 1st century BC. In his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shiji" title="Shiji">Shiji</a></i> published in 94 BC, Chinese historian <a href="/wiki/Sima_Qian" title="Sima Qian">Sima Qian</a> remarked that "the largest of these embassies to foreign states numbered several hundred persons, while even the smaller parties included over 100 members&#160;... In the course of one year anywhere from five to six to over ten parties would be sent out."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> In terms of the silk trade, the Sogdians also served as the primary middlemen between the Chinese Han Empire and the <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian Empire</a> of the Middle East and West Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-48">[48]</a></sup> Sogdians played a major role in facilitating trade between China and Central Asia along the Silk Roads as late as the 10th century, their language serving as a <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> for Asian trade as far back as the 4th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="1199" data-file-width="760" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png/170px-ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png/255px-ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png/340px-ForeignerWithWineskin-Earthenware-TangDynasty-ROM-May8-08.png 2x" width="170"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:132px;max-width:132px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="731" data-file-width="348" decoding="async" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg/130px-ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg/195px-ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg/260px-ForeignMerchant_at_the_silk_road.jpg 2x" width="130"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: <i><a href="/wiki/Sancai" title="Sancai">Sancai</a></i>-glazed figurine depicting a Sogdian holding a <a href="/wiki/Wineskin" title="Wineskin">wineskin</a>, Chinese <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a>, c. 675&#8211;750 AD<br/> <b>Right image</b>: <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">ceramic figurine</a> of a Sogdian merchant in northern China, Tang Dynasty, 7th century AD</div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1206" data-file-width="1233" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG/150px-SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG/225px-SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG/300px-SogdianCoin6thCentury.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1452" data-file-width="1430" decoding="async" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg/150px-ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg/225px-ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg/300px-ChineseShapedSogdianCoinKelpin8thCenturyCE.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: Sogdian coin, 6th century, <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a><br/> <b>Right image</b>: <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">Chinese-influenced</a> Sogdian coin, from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kelpin" title="Kelpin">Kelpin</a>, 8th century, British Museum</div></div></div>\n<p>Subsequent to their domination by Alexander the Great, the Sogdians from the city of Marakanda (<a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>) became dominant as traveling merchants, occupying a key position along the ancient Silk Road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> They played an active role in the spread of faiths such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manicheism" title="Manicheism">Manicheism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> along the Silk Road. <a href="/wiki/Chinese_historiography" title="Chinese historiography">The Chinese</a> <i>Sui Shu</i> (<i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Sui" title="Book of Sui">Book of Sui</a></i>) describes Sogdians as "skilled merchants" who attracted many foreign traders to their land to engage in commerce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p134_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p134-52">[52]</a></sup> They were described by the Chinese as born merchants, learning their commercial skills at an early age. It appears from sources, such as documents found by Sir <a href="/wiki/Aurel_Stein" title="Aurel Stein">Aurel Stein</a> and others, that by the 4th century they may have monopolized trade <a href="/wiki/China%E2%80%93India_relations" title="China&#8211;India relations">between India and China</a>. A letter written by Sogdian merchants dated 313 AD and found in the ruins of a watchtower in <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> was intended to be sent to merchants in <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Samarkand" title="Timeline of Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, warning them that after <a href="/wiki/Liu_Cong_(Han_Zhao)" title="Liu Cong (Han Zhao)">Liu Cong</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Han_Zhao" title="Han Zhao">Han Zhao</a> sacked <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(265%E2%80%93420)" title="Jin dynasty (265&#8211;420)">Jin emperor</a> fled the capital, there was no worthwhile business there for Indian and Sogdian merchants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p3_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p3-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_pp133-34_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_pp133-34-53">[53]</a></sup> Furthermore, in 568 AD a Turko-Sogdian delegation travelled to the Roman emperor in Constantinople to obtain permission to trade and in the following years commercial activity between the states flourished.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> Put simply, the Sogdians dominated trade along the Silk Road from the 2nd century BC until the 10th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_43-1"><a href="#cite_note-wood-43">[43]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Suyab" title="Suyab">Suyab</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taraz" title="Taraz">Talas</a> in modern-day <a href="/wiki/Kyrgyzstan" title="Kyrgyzstan">Kyrgyzstan</a> were the main Sogdian centers in the north that dominated the caravan routes of the 6th to 8th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> Their commercial interests were protected by the resurgent military power of the <a href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kt%C3%BCrks" title="G&#246;kt&#252;rks">G&#246;kt&#252;rks</a>, whose empire was built on the political power of the <a href="/wiki/Ashina_tribe" title="Ashina tribe">Ashina</a> clan and economic clout of the Sogdians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranica_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Sogdian trade, with some interruptions, continued into the 9th century. For instance, camels, women, girls, silver, and gold were seized from Sogdia during a raid by <a href="/wiki/Qapaghan_Qaghan" title="Qapaghan Qaghan">Qapaghan Qaghan</a> (692&#8211;716), ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Turkic_Khaganate" title="Second Turkic Khaganate">Second Turkic Khaganate</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> In the 10th century Sogdiana was incorporated into the <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate" title="Uyghur Khaganate">Uighur Empire</a>, which <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Qocho" title="Kingdom of Qocho">until 840</a> encompassed northern Central Asia. This <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khaganate" title="Khaganate">khaganate</a> obtained enormous deliveries of silk from Tang China in exchange for horses, in turn relying on the Sogdians to sell much of this silk further west.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2001_p169_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2001_p169-60">[60]</a></sup> Peter B. Golden writes that the <a href="/wiki/Uyghurs" title="Uyghurs">Uyghurs</a> not only adopted the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_alphabet" title="Sogdian alphabet">writing system</a> and religious faiths of the Sogdians, such as Manichaeism, Buddhism, and Christianity, but also looked to the Sogdians as "mentors" while gradually replacing them in their roles as Silk Road traders and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bezeklik_Thousand_Buddha_Caves" title="Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves">purveyors of culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography and cartography in medieval Islam">Muslim geographers</a> of the 10th century drew upon Sogdian records dating to 750&#8211;840. After the end of <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Uyghur_people" title="History of the Uyghur people">the Uyghur Empire</a>, Sogdian trade underwent a crisis. Following the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">Muslim conquest of Transoxiana</a> in the 8th century, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Samanid_dynasty" title="Samanid dynasty">Samanids</a> resumed trade on the northwestern road leading to the <a href="/wiki/Khazars" title="Khazars">Khazars</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urals" title="Urals">Urals</a> and the northeastern one toward the nearby Turkic tribes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Iranica_57-1"><a href="#cite_note-Iranica-57">[57]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 5th and 6th century many Sogdians took up residence in the <a href="/wiki/Hexi_Corridor" title="Hexi Corridor">Hexi Corridor</a> where they retained autonomy in terms of governance and had a designated official administrator known as a <i>sabao</i>, which suggests their importance to the socioeconomic structure of China. The Sogdian influence on trade in China is also made apparent by a Chinese document which lists taxes paid on caravan trade in the <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a> region and shows that twenty-nine out of the thirty-five commercial transactions involved Sogdian merchants, and in thirteen of those cases both the buyer and the seller were Sogdian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Trade goods brought to China included <a href="/wiki/Grape" title="Grape">grapes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alfalfa" title="Alfalfa">alfalfa</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sassanid_Empire" title="Sassanid Empire">Sassanian silverware</a>, as well as glass containers, Mediterranean coral, brass Buddhist images, Roman wool cloth, and <a href="/wiki/Amber_Road" title="Amber Road">Baltic amber</a>. These were exchanged for Chinese paper, copper, and silk.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wood_43-2"><a href="#cite_note-wood-43">[43]</a></sup> In the 7th century the Chinese Buddhist pilgrim <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> noted with approval that Sogdian boys were taught to read and write at the age of five, though their skill was turned to trade, disappointing the scholarly Xuanzang. He also recorded the Sogdians working in other capacities such as farmers, carpetweavers, glassmakers, and woodcarvers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trade_and_diplomacy_with_the_Byzantine_Empire">Trade and diplomacy with the Byzantine Empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Trade and diplomacy with the Byzantine Empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/First_Perso-Turkic_War" title="First Perso-Turkic War">First Perso-Turkic War</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine%E2%80%93Sasanian_wars" title="Byzantine&#8211;Sasanian wars">Byzantine&#8211;Sasanian wars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_silk" title="Byzantine silk">Byzantine silk</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_warriors" title="Sogdian warriors">Sogdian warriors</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Sino-Roman relations</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine-Mongol_alliance" title="Byzantine-Mongol alliance">Byzantine-Mongol alliance</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">Europeans in Medieval China</a></div>\n<p>Historical knowledge about Sogdia is somewhat hazy during the period of the Parthian Empire (247 BC &#8211; 224 AD) in Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p5_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p5-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1217_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1217-65">[65]</a></sup> The subsequent <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">Sasanian Empire</a> of Persia conquered and incorporated Sogdia as a satrapy in 260,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p5_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p5-64">[64]</a></sup> an inscription dating to the reign of <a href="/wiki/Shapur_I" title="Shapur I">Shapur I</a> noting that its limits formed the northeastern Sasanian borderlands with the Kushan Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1217_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1217-65">[65]</a></sup> However, by the 5th century the region was captured by the rival <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalite_Empire" title="Hephthalite Empire">Hephthalite Empire</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p5_64-2"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p5-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Shortly after the <a href="/wiki/Smuggling_of_silkworm_eggs_into_the_Byzantine_Empire" title="Smuggling of silkworm eggs into the Byzantine Empire">smuggling of silkworm eggs into the Byzantine Empire</a> from China by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christian" title="Nestorian Christian">Nestorian Christian</a> monks, the 6th-century Byzantine historian <a href="/wiki/Menander_Protector" title="Menander Protector">Menander Protector</a> writes of how the Sogdians attempted to establish a direct trade of Chinese <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>. After forming an alliance with the Sasanian ruler <a href="/wiki/Khosrow_I" title="Khosrow I">Khosrow I</a> to defeat the Hephthalite Empire, <a href="/wiki/Ist%C3%A4mi" title="Ist&#228;mi">Ist&#228;mi</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kt%C3%BCrk" title="G&#246;kt&#252;rk">G&#246;kt&#252;rk</a> ruler of the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_Khaganate" title="Turkic Khaganate">Turkic Khaganate</a>, was approached by Sogdian merchants requesting permission to seek an audience with the Sassanid king of kings for the privilege of traveling through Persian territories in order to trade with the Byzantines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-48">[48]</a></sup> Ist&#228;mi refused the first request, but when he sanctioned the second one and had the Sogdian embassy sent to the Sassanid king, the latter had the members of the embassy poisoned.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-48">[48]</a></sup> Maniah, a Sogdian diplomat, convinced Ist&#228;mi to send an embassy directly to Byzantium\'s capital <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, which arrived in 568 and offered not only silk as a gift to Byzantine ruler <a href="/wiki/Justin_II" title="Justin II">Justin II</a>, but also proposed an alliance against Sassanid Persia. Justin II agreed and sent an embassy to the Turkic Khaganate, ensuring the direct silk trade desired by the Sogdians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p133_48-3"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p133-48">[48]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2001_p168_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2001_p168-66">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p9_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p9-67">[67]</a></sup>\n</p><p>It appears, however, that direct trade with the Sogdians remained limited in light of the small amount of <a href="/wiki/Roman_currency" title="Roman currency">Roman</a> and <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_coinage" title="Byzantine coinage">Byzantine coins</a> found in Central Asian and Chinese archaeological sites belonging to this era. Although <a href="/wiki/Sino-Roman_relations" title="Sino-Roman relations">Roman embassies</a> apparently reached Han China from 166 AD onwards,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Rome" title="Ancient Rome">ancient Romans</a> imported Han Chinese silk while the Han-dynasty Chinese imported <a href="/wiki/Roman_glass" title="Roman glass">Roman glasswares</a> as discovered in their tombs,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Valerie_Hansen" title="Valerie Hansen">Valerie Hansen</a> (2012) wrote that no Roman coins from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (507&#8211;27 BC) or the <a href="/wiki/Principate" title="Principate">Principate</a> (27 BC &#8211; 330 AD) era of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a> have been found in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p97_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p97-71">[71]</a></sup> However, <a href="/wiki/Warwick_Ball" title="Warwick Ball">Warwick Ball</a> (2016) upends this notion by pointing to a hoard of sixteen Roman coins found at <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>, China (formerly <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a>), dated to the reigns of various emperors from <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> (14&#8211;37 AD) to <a href="/wiki/Aurelian" title="Aurelian">Aurelian</a> (270&#8211;275 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ball_2016_p154_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-ball_2016_p154-72">[72]</a></sup> The earliest gold <a href="/wiki/Solidus_(coin)" title="Solidus (coin)"><i>solidus</i> coins</a> from the Eastern Roman Empire found in China date to the reign of Byzantine emperor <a href="/wiki/Theodosius_II" title="Theodosius II">Theodosius II</a> (r. 408&#8211;450) and altogether only forty-eight of them have been found (compared to thirteen-hundred silver coins) in <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a> and the rest of China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p97_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p97-71">[71]</a></sup> The use of silver coins in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a> persisted long after the <a href="/wiki/Tang_campaign_against_Karakhoja" title="Tang campaign against Karakhoja">Tang campaign against Karakhoja</a> and Chinese conquest of 640, with a gradual adoption of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">Chinese bronze coinage</a> over the course of the 7th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p97_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p97-71">[71]</a></sup> The fact that these Eastern Roman coins were almost always found with <a href="/wiki/Monetary_history_of_Iran" title="Monetary history of Iran">Sasanian Persian silver coins</a> and Eastern Roman gold coins were used more as ceremonial objects like <a href="/wiki/Talisman" title="Talisman">talismans</a> confirms the pre-eminent importance of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Iran" title="Greater Iran">Greater Iran</a> in Chinese Silk Road commerce of Central Asia compared to Eastern Rome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Sogdian_merchants.2C_generals.2C_and_statesmen_of_Imperial_China"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Sogdian_merchants,_generals,_and_statesmen_of_Imperial_China">Sogdian merchants, generals, and statesmen of Imperial China</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Sogdian merchants, generals, and statesmen of Imperial China">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_in_Chinese_history" title="Ethnic groups in Chinese history">Ethnic groups in Chinese history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_minorities_in_China" title="Ethnic minorities in China">Ethnic minorities in China</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">Western Regions</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:418px;max-width:418px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="323" data-file-width="406" decoding="async" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg/200px-BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg/300px-BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg/400px-BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:212px;max-width:212px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch,_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="158" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch%2C_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg/210px-Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch%2C_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch%2C_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg/315px-Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch%2C_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch%2C_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg/420px-Anjia_tomb_gate_and_couch%2C_Shaanxi_History_Museum.jpg 2x" width="210"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: kneeling Sogdian donors to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> (fresco, with detail), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bezeklik_Thousand_Buddha_Caves" title="Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves">Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves</a>, near <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a> in the eastern <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a>, China, 8th century <br/><b>Right image</b>: the stone tomb gate and couch of An Jia (&#23433;&#20285;), a <a href="/wiki/Northern_Zhou" title="Northern Zhou">Northern Zhou</a> (557&#8211;581 AD) period Sogdian nobleman,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> excavated from <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Xi%27an" title="Xi\'an">Xi\'an</a>), China; An Jia held the title of Sar-pav of <a href="/wiki/Tong_Prefecture_(Shaanxi)" title="Tong Prefecture (Shaanxi)">Tongzhou prefecture</a> and was in charge of commercial affairs of <a href="/wiki/Cities_along_the_Silk_Road" title="Cities along the Silk Road">foreign merchants from Middle Asia</a>, who made businesses in China; the stone gate is flanked by two lions and the horizontal tablet is carved with a <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">sacrificial scene</a> in accordance with <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">Zoroastrianism</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Aside from the Sogdians of Central Asia who acted as middlemen in the Silk Road trade, other Sogdians settled down in China for generations. Although many Sogdians had fled Luoyang following the collapse of the Jin Dynasty\'s control over northern China in 311 AD, some Sogdians continued living in Gansu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_pp133-34_53-1"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_pp133-34-53">[53]</a></sup> Sogdian families living in Gansu created funerary <a href="/wiki/Epitaph" title="Epitaph">epitaphs</a> explaining the history of their illustrious houses. For instance, a <i>sabao</i> (&#34217;&#20445;, from <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> <i>sarthavaha</i>, meaning caravan leader)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2001_p168_66-1"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2001_p168-66">[66]</a></sup> from Anxi (western Sogdiana or <a href="/wiki/Parthia" title="Parthia">Parthia</a>) who lived in Jiuquan during the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Wei" title="Northern Wei">Northern Wei</a> (386 &#8211; 535 AD), was the ancestor of An Tugen, a man who rose from a common merchant to become a top ranking minister of state for the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Qi" title="Northern Qi">Northern Qi</a> (550 &#8211; 577 AD).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p134_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p134-52">[52]</a></sup> Valerie Hansen asserts that around this time and extending into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> (618 &#8211; 907 AD), the Sogdians "became the most influential of the non-Chinese groups resident in China," settling throughout Chinese territory, marrying Chinese women, purchasing land, with newcomers living there permanently instead of returning to their homelands in Sogdiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p134_52-2"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p134-52">[52]</a></sup> They were concentrated in large numbers around Luoyang and Chang\'an, but also <a href="/wiki/Xiangyang" title="Xiangyang">Xiangyang</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hubei" title="Hubei">Hubei</a>, building <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zoroastrian" title="Zoroastrian">Zoroastrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Fire_temple" title="Fire temple">temples</a> to service their communities once they reached the threshold of roughly 100 households.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p134_52-3"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p134-52">[52]</a></sup> From the Northern Qi to Tang periods, the leaders of these communities, the <i>sabao</i>, were incorporated into the official hierarchy of state officials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p134_52-4"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p134-52">[52]</a></sup> Their burial practices blended both Chinese forms such as carved funerary beds with Zoroastrian
