sensibilities in mind, such as separating the body from both the earth and water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="470" decoding="async" height="277" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/220px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/330px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg/440px-Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Central_Asian_Buddhist_Monks.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_monk" title="Buddhist monk">Buddhist monks</a> on a mural of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bezeklik_Thousand_Buddha_Caves" title="Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves">Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves</a> near <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, China, 9th century AD. <a href="/wiki/Albert_von_Le_Coq" title="Albert von Le Coq">Albert von Le Coq</a> (1913) assumed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blue_eyes" title="Blue eyes">blue-eyed</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_hair" title="Red hair">red-haired</a> monk was a <a href="/wiki/Tocharians" title="Tocharians">Tocharian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> modern scholarship however identified similar <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">Caucasian figures</a> of <a href="/wiki/File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg" title="File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg">the same cave temple</a> (No. 9) as ethnic Sogdians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gasparini_2014_pp134-163_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-gasparini_2014_pp134-163-77">[77]</a></sup> who were a minority in Turpan during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> in 7th&#8211;8th century and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Qocho" title="Kingdom of Qocho">Uyghur rule</a> (9th&#8211;13th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>In addition to being merchants, monks, and government officials, Sogdians also served as soldiers in the Tang military.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p135_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p135-79">[79]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a>, whose father was Sogdian and mother a Gokturk, rose to the position of a military governor (<i><a href="/wiki/Jiedushi" title="Jiedushi">jiedushi</a></i>) in the northeast before leading the <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a> (755 &#8211; 763 AD), which split the loyalties of the Sogdians in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p135_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p135-79">[79]</a></sup> The An Lushan rebellion was supported by many Sogdians, and in its aftermath many of them were slain or changed their names to escape their Sogdian heritage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> so that little is known about the Sogdian presence in North China since that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> Sogdians continued as active traders in China following the defeat of the rebellion, but many of them were compelled to hide their ethnic identity. A prominent case was An Chongzhang, Minister of War, and Duke of Liang who, in 756, asked <a href="/wiki/Emperor_Suzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Suzong of Tang">Emperor Suzong of Tang</a> to allow him to change his name to <a href="/wiki/Li_Baoyu" title="Li Baoyu">Li Baoyu</a> because of his shame in sharing <a href="/wiki/An_(surname)" title="An (surname)">the same surname</a> with the rebel leader.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p135_79-2"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p135-79">[79]</a></sup> This change of surnames was enacted retroactively for all of his family members, so that his ancestors would also be bestowed the <a href="/wiki/Li_(surname)" title="Li (surname)">surname Li</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p135_79-3"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p135-79">[79]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Tang and subsequent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Five_Dynasties_and_Ten_Kingdoms" title="Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms">Five Dynasties</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Song_Dynasty" title="Song Dynasty">Song Dynasty</a>, a large community of Sogdians also existed in the multicultural <i><a href="/wiki/Entrep%C3%B4t" title="Entrep&#244;t">entrep&#244;t</a></i> of Dunhuang, Gansu, a major center of Buddhist learning and home to the Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Mogao_Caves" title="Mogao Caves">Mogao Caves</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Although Dunhuang and the Hexi Corridor were captured by the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a> after the An Lushan Rebellion, in 848 the ethnic Han Chinese general <a href="/wiki/Zhang_Yichao" title="Zhang Yichao">Zhang Yichao</a> (799&#8211;872) managed to wrestle control of the region from <a href="/wiki/Era_of_Fragmentation" title="Era of Fragmentation">the Tibetans during their civil war</a>, establishing the <a href="/wiki/Guiyi_Circuit" title="Guiyi Circuit">Guiyi Circuit</a> under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emperor_Xu%C4%81nzong_of_Tang" title="Emperor Xu&#257;nzong of Tang">Emperor Xu&#257;nzong of Tang</a> (r. 846&#8211;859).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ZZTJ249_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-ZZTJ249-84">[84]</a></sup> Although the region occasionally fell under the rule of different states, it retained its multilingual nature as evidenced by an abundance of manuscripts (religious and secular) in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tibetan_languages" title="Tibetan languages">Tibetan</a>, but also <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">Sogdian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Saka_language" title="Saka language">Khotanese</a> (another <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_language" title="Eastern Iranian language">Eastern Iranian language</a> native to <a href="/wiki/Western_Regions" title="Western Regions">the region</a>), <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">Uyghur</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>\n</p><p>From the <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">Chinese surnames</a> listed in the <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang_manuscripts" title="Dunhuang manuscripts">Tang-era Dunhuang manuscript</a> Pelliot chinois 3319V (containing the following text: &#30707;&#23450;&#20449;&#21491;&#20840;&#30707;&#19985;&#23376;&#30707;&#23450;&#22900;&#31119;&#24310;&#31119;&#20840;&#20445;&#26124;&#24373;&#19985;&#23376;&#26446;&#21315;&#23376;&#26446;&#23450;&#20449;), the names of the Nine Zhaowu Clans (or the "nine jeweled surnames" &#26157;&#27494;&#20061;&#22995;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup> the prominent ethnic Sogdian families of China, have been deduced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> Of these the most common Sogdian surname throughout China was <a href="/wiki/Shi_(surname)" title="Shi (surname)">Shi</a> (&#30707;, generally given to those from Chach, modern <a href="/wiki/Tashkent" title="Tashkent">Tashkent</a>), whereas the surnames Shi (&#21490;, from Kesh, modern <a href="/wiki/Shahrisabz" title="Shahrisabz">Shahrisabz</a>), <a href="/wiki/An_(surname)" title="An (surname)">An</a> (&#23433;, from Bukhara), <a href="/wiki/Mi_(surname)" title="Mi (surname)">Mi</a> (&#31859;, from <a href="/wiki/Panjakent" title="Panjakent">Panjakent</a>), <a href="/wiki/Kang_(Chinese_surname)" title="Kang (Chinese surname)">Kang</a> (&#24247;, from Samarkand), <a href="/wiki/Cao_(Chinese_surname)" title="Cao (Chinese surname)">Cao</a> (&#26361;, from Kabudhan, north of the <a href="/wiki/Zeravshan_River" title="Zeravshan River">Zeravshan River</a>), and <a href="/wiki/He_(surname)" title="He (surname)">He</a> (&#20309;, from Kushaniyah) appear frequently in Dunhuang manuscripts and registers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-2"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The influence of <a href="/wiki/Sinicization" title="Sinicization">Sinicized</a> and multilingual Sogdians during this <i>Guiyijun</i> (&#27512;&#32681;&#36557;) period (c. 850 &#8211; c. 1000 AD) of Dunhuang is evident in a large number of manuscripts written in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">Chinese characters</a> from left to right instead of vertically, mirroring the direction of how the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_alphabet" title="Sogdian alphabet">Sogdian alphabet</a> is read.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> Sogdians of Dunhuang also commonly formed and joined lay associations among their local communities, convening at Sogdian-owned <a href="/wiki/Tavern" title="Tavern">taverns</a> in scheduled meetings mentioned in their <a href="/wiki/Epistle" title="Epistle">epistolary letters</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Sogdians living in Turfan under the Tang dynasty and <a href="/wiki/Gaochang" title="Gaochang">Gaochang Kingdom</a> engaged in a variety of occupations that included: farming, military service, painting, <a href="/wiki/Leather_crafting" title="Leather crafting">leather crafting</a> and selling products such as iron goods.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-3"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup> The Sogdians had been migrating to Turfan since the 4th century, yet the pace of migration began to climb steadily with the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Persia" title="Muslim conquest of Persia">Muslim conquest of Persia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Sasanian_Empire" title="Fall of the Sasanian Empire">Fall of the Sasanian Empire</a> in 651, followed by the Islamic conquest of Samarkand in 712.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-4"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arab_Muslim_conquest_of_Central_Asia">Arab Muslim conquest of Central Asia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Arab Muslim conquest of Central Asia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Muslim_conquest_of_Transoxiana" title="Muslim conquest of Transoxiana">Muslim conquest of Transoxiana</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Asad_ibn_Abdallah_al-Qasri" title="Asad ibn Abdallah al-Qasri">Asad ibn Abdallah al-Qasri</a></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:398px;max-width:398px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:292px;max-width:292px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lions,_soie_polychrome_sogdienne,_Asie_centrale.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3184" data-file-width="4257" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg/290px-Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg/435px-Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg/580px-Lions%2C_soie_polychrome_sogdienne%2C_Asie_centrale.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:102px;max-width:102px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="3722" data-file-width="1704" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg/100px-Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg/150px-Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg/200px-Caftan_MET_DT1115.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: a lion <a href="/wiki/Motif_(textile_arts)" title="Motif (textile arts)">motif</a> on Sogdian <a href="/wiki/Polychrome" title="Polychrome">polychrome</a> <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, 8th century AD, most likely from <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> <br/><b>Right image</b>: a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caftan" title="Caftan">caftan</a> worn by a horseman along the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a>, 8th&#8211;10th century AD, <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art" title="Metropolitan Museum of Art">Metropolitan Museum of Art</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Qutayba_ibn_Muslim" title="Qutayba ibn Muslim">Qutayba ibn Muslim</a> (669&#8211;716), Governor of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Khorasan" title="Greater Khorasan">Greater Khorasan</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_Caliphate" title="Umayyad Caliphate">Umayyad Caliphate</a> (661&#8211;750), initiated the Muslim conquest of Sogdia during the early 8th century, with the local ruler of <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a> offering him aid as an Umayyad ally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1217_65-2"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1217-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> However, when his successor <a href="/wiki/Al-Jarrah_ibn_Abdallah" title="Al-Jarrah ibn Abdallah">Al-Jarrah ibn Abdallah</a> governed Khorosan (717&#8211;719), many native Sogdians, who had converted to Islam, began to revolt when they were no longer exempt from paying the tax on non-Muslims, the <i><a href="/wiki/Jizya" title="Jizya">jizya</a>,</i> because of a new law stating that proof of <a href="/wiki/Circumcision" title="Circumcision">circumcision</a> and literacy in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> was necessary for new converts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1217_65-3"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1217-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-litvinski_et_al_1999_p459_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-litvinski_et_al_1999_p459-91">[91]</a></sup> With the aid of Turkic peoples, the Sogdians were able to expel the Umayyad Arab garrison from Samarkand and Umayyad attempts to restore power there were rebuffed until the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Sa%27id_ibn_Amr_al-Harashi" title="Sa\'id ibn Amr al-Harashi">Sa\'id ibn Amr al-Harashi</a> (fl. 720&#8211;735). The Sogdian ruler (i.e. <i><a href="/wiki/Ikhshid" title="Ikhshid">ikhshid</a></i>) of Samarkand, <a href="/wiki/Gurak" title="Gurak">Gurak</a>, who had previously overthrown the pro-Umayyad Sogdian ruler <a href="/wiki/Tarkhun" title="Tarkhun">Tarkhun</a> in 710, decided that resistance against al-Harashi\'s large Arab force was pointless and thereafter persuaded his followers to declare allegiance to the Umayyad governor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-litvinski_et_al_1999_p459_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-litvinski_et_al_1999_p459-91">[91]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Divashtich" title="Divashtich">Divashtich</a> (r. 706&#8211;722), the Sogdian ruler of <a href="/wiki/Panjakent" title="Panjakent">Panjakent</a>, led his forces to the <a href="/wiki/Zarafshan_Range" title="Zarafshan Range">Zarafshan Range</a> (near modern <a href="/wiki/Zarafshan,_Tajikistan" title="Zarafshan, Tajikistan">Zarafshan, Tajikistan</a>), whereas the Sogdians following Karzanj, the ruler of Pai (modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kattakurgan,_Uzbekistan" title="Kattakurgan, Uzbekistan">Kattakurgan, Uzbekistan</a>), fled to the <a href="/wiki/Principality_of_Farghana" title="Principality of Farghana">Principality of Farghana</a>, where their ruler at-Tar (or Alutar) promised them safety and refuge from the Umayyads. However, at-Tar secretly informed al-Harashi of the Sogdians hiding in <a href="/wiki/Khujand" title="Khujand">Khujand</a>, who were then slaughtered by al-Harashi\'s forces after their arrival.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg/200px-Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg/300px-Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg/400px-Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Westerner_on_a_camel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramic" title="Chinese ceramic">Chinese ceramic</a> statuette of a Sogdian merchant riding on a <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_camel" title="Bactrian camel">Bactrian camel</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Umayyads fell in 750 to the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>, which quickly asserted itself in Central Asia after winning the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Talas" title="Battle of Talas">Battle of Talas</a> (along the <a href="/wiki/Talas_River" title="Talas River">Talas River</a> in modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Talas_Oblast" title="Talas Oblast">Talas Oblast</a>, Kyrgyzstan) in 751 against the Chinese Tang Dynasty. This conflict  incidentally introduced Chinese <a href="/wiki/Papermaking" title="Papermaking">papermaking</a> to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_world" title="Islamic world">Islamic world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanks_2010_p4_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-hanks_2010_p4-93">[93]</a></sup> The cultural consequences and political ramifications of this battle meant the <a href="/wiki/Protectorate_General_to_Pacify_the_West" title="Protectorate General to Pacify the West">retreat of the Chinese empire from Central Asia</a>. It also allowed for the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Samanid_Empire" title="Samanid Empire">Samanid Empire</a> (819&#8211;999), a Persian state centered at Bukhara (in what is now modern <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>) that nominally observed the Abbasids as their <a href="/wiki/Overlord" title="Overlord">overlords</a>, yet retained a great deal of autonomy and upheld the mercantile legacy of the Sogdians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanks_2010_p4_93-1"><a href="#cite_note-hanks_2010_p4-93">[93]</a></sup> Yet the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">Sogdian language</a> gradually declined in favor of the <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian language</a> of the Samanids (the ancestor to the modern <a href="/wiki/Tajik_language" title="Tajik language">Tajik language</a>), the spoken language of renowned poets and intellectuals of the age such as <a href="/wiki/Ferdowsi" title="Ferdowsi">Ferdowsi</a> (940&#8211;1020).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanks_2010_p4_93-2"><a href="#cite_note-hanks_2010_p4-93">[93]</a></sup> So too did the original religions of the Sogdians decline; Zoroastrianism, Buddhism, <a href="/wiki/Manichaeism" title="Manichaeism">Manichaeism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nestorian_Christianity" title="Nestorian Christianity">Nestorian Christianity</a> disappeared in the region by the end of the Samanid period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hanks_2010_p4_93-3"><a href="#cite_note-hanks_2010_p4-93">[93]</a></sup> The Samanids were also responsible for converting the surrounding <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a> to <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, which presaged the conquest of their empire in 999 by an Islamic Turkic power, the <a href="/wiki/Kara-Khanid_Khanate" title="Kara-Khanid Khanate">Kara-Khanid Khanate</a> (840&#8211;1212).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the early 13th century <a href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_Khwarezmia" title="Mongol conquest of Khwarezmia">Khwarezmia was invaded</a> by the early <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> and its ruler <a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan" title="Genghis Khan">Genghis Khan</a> destroyed the once vibrant cities of Bukhara and Samarkand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> However, in 1370 Samarkand saw a revival as the capital of the <a href="/wiki/Timurid_Empire" title="Timurid Empire">Timurid Empire</a>. The Turko-Mongol ruler <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a> forcefully brought artisans and intellectuals from across Asia to Samarkand, transforming it not only into a trade hub but also one of the most important cities of the Islamic world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_culture">Language and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Language and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<p>The 6th century is thought to be the peak of Sogdian culture, judging by its highly developed artistic tradition. By this point, the Sogdians were entrenched in their role as the central Asian traveling and trading merchants, transferring goods, culture and religion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Zarafshan_Range" title="Zarafshan Range">valley of the Zarafshan</a> around Samarkand retained its Sogdian name, Samarkand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_britannica_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia_britannica-8">[8]</a></sup> According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Encyclop%C3%A6dia_Britannica_Eleventh_Edition" title="Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica Eleventh Edition">Encyclop&#230;dia Britannica</a></i>, medieval <a href="/wiki/Geography_and_cartography_in_medieval_Islam" title="Geography and cartography in medieval Islam">Arab geographers</a> considered it one of the four fairest regions of the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-encyclopedia_britannica_8-2"><a href="#cite_note-encyclopedia_britannica-8">[8]</a></sup> Where the Sogdians moved in considerable numbers, their language  made a considerable impact. For instance, <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Han_dynasty" title="History of the Han dynasty">during China\'s Han dynasty</a>, the native name of the Tarim Basin city-state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loulan" title="Loulan">Loulan</a> was "Kroraina," possibly <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">from Greek</a> due to <a href="/wiki/Indo-Greek_Kingdom" title="Indo-Greek Kingdom">nearby Hellenistic influence</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> However, centuries later in 664 AD the Tang Chinese Buddhist monk <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> labelled it as "Nafupo" (&#32013;&#32283;&#28325;), which according to Dr. Hisao Matsuda is a transliteration of the Sogdian word <i>Navapa</i> meaning "new water."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Afrasiab_painting" title="Afrasiab painting">Afrasiab paintings</a> of the 6th to 7th centuries in Samarkand, Uzbekistan offer a rare surviving example of Sogdian art. The paintings, showing scenes of daily life and events such as the arrival of foreign ambassadors, are located within the ruins of aristocratic homes. It is unclear if any of these palatial residences served as the official palace of the rulers of Samarkand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The oldest surviving Sogdian monumental wall murals date to the 5th century and are located at Panjakent, Tajikistan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> In addition to revealing aspects of their social and political lives, Sogdian art has also been instrumental in aiding historians\' understanding of their religious beliefs. For instance, it is clear that Buddhist Sogdians incorporated some of their own <a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Persian deities</a> into their version of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist_Pantheon" title="Buddhist Pantheon">Buddhist Pantheon</a>. At <a href="/wiki/Zhetysu" title="Zhetysu">Zhetysu</a>, Sogdian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gilded" title="Gilded">gilded</a> bronze plaques on a <a href="/wiki/Buddhist_temple" title="Buddhist temple">Buddhist temple</a> show a pairing of a male and female deity with outstretched hands holding a miniature <a href="/wiki/Camel" title="Camel">camel</a>, a common non-Buddhist image similarly found in the paintings of Samarkand and Panjakent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Language">Language</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Language">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:328px;max-width:328px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bugut.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1229" data-file-width="922" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bugut.jpg/170px-Bugut.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bugut.jpg/255px-Bugut.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Bugut.jpg/340px-Bugut.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="903" data-file-width="600" decoding="async" height="226" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/150px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/225px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6f/Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg/300px-Chinese_Slave_trade.jpg 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: The "Bugut" inscription of <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a>, written shortly after 581 AD in the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_alphabet" title="Sogdian alphabet">Sogdian alphabet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tafazzoli_2003_p323_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-tafazzoli_2003_p323-103">[103]</a></sup> and commissioned by the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_Khaganate" title="Turkic Khaganate">Turkic Khaganate</a> to relate the history of their ruling <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gokturk" title="Gokturk">Gokturk</a> <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">khans</a><br/> <b>Right image</b>: a contract <a href="/wiki/Chinese_characters" title="Chinese characters">written in Chinese</a> from the Tang dynasty in <a href="/wiki/Turpan" title="Turpan">Turpan</a> that records the purchase of a 15-year-old slave for six bolts of plain silk and five <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Chinese_coinage" title="Ancient Chinese coinage">Chinese coins</a>, dated 661 AD</div></div></div>\n<p>The Sogdians spoke an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Iranian_languages" title="Eastern Iranian languages">Eastern Iranian</a> language called Sogdian, closely related to <a href="/wiki/Bactrian_language" title="Bactrian language">Bactrian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezmian_language" title="Khwarezmian language">Khwarazmian</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Khotan" title="Kingdom of Khotan">Khotanese</a> <a href="/wiki/Saka_language" title="Saka language">language Saka</a>, widely spoken Eastern Iranian languages of Central Asia in ancient times.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p5_64-3"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p5-64">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tafazzoli_2003_p323_103-1"><a href="#cite_note-tafazzoli_2003_p323-103">[103]</a></sup> Sogdian was also prominent in the <a href="/wiki/Oasis" title="Oasis">oasis</a> city-state of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tarim_Basin" title="Tarim Basin">Tarim Basin</a> region of <a href="/wiki/Northwest_China" title="Northwest China">Northwest China</a> (in modern <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tafazzoli_2003_p323_103-2"><a href="#cite_note-tafazzoli_2003_p323-103">[103]</a></sup> Judging by the Sogdian <i>Bugut</i> inscription of <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> written c. 581, the Sogdian language was also an official language of the <a href="/wiki/Turkic_Khaganate" title="Turkic Khaganate">Turkic Khaganate</a> established by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gokturks" title="Gokturks">Gokturks</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_1981_p9_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_1981_p9-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tafazzoli_2003_p323_103-3"><a href="#cite_note-tafazzoli_2003_p323-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sogdian was written largely in three scripts: the <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_alphabet" title="Sogdian alphabet">Sogdian alphabet</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Syriac_alphabet" title="Syriac alphabet">Syriac alphabet</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Manichaean_alphabet" title="Manichaean alphabet">Manichaean alphabet</a>, each derived from the <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_alphabet" title="Aramaic alphabet">Aramaic alphabet</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> which had been widely used in both the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Achaemenid</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parthian_Empire" title="Parthian Empire">Parthian</a> empires of ancient Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-baumer_2012_pp202-203_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-baumer_2012_pp202-203-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup> The Sogdian alphabet formed the basis of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Uyghur_alphabet" title="Old Uyghur alphabet">Old Uyghur alphabet</a> of the 8th century, which in turn was used to create the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_script" title="Mongolian script">Mongolian script</a> of the early <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> during the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Yaghnobi_people" title="Yaghnobi people">Yaghnobi people</a> living in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sughd" title="Sughd">Sughd</a> province of <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> still speak <a href="/wiki/Yaghnobi_language" title="Yaghnobi language">a dialect of the Sogdian language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1217_65-4"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1217-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Yaghnobi is largely a continuation of the medieval Sogdian dialect from the <a href="/wiki/Osrushana" title="Osrushana">Osrushana</a> region of the western <a href="/wiki/Fergana_Valley" title="Fergana Valley">Fergana Valley</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> The great majority of the Sogdian people assimilated with other local groups such as the Bactrians, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kwaresm" title="Kwaresm">Chorasmians</a>, and in particular with <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a> and came to speak Persian. In 819 the Persians founded the Samanid Empire in the region. They are among the ancestors of the modern <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/T%C4%81jik_people" title="T&#257;jik people">Tajiks</a>. Numerous Sogdian <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> can be found in the modern Tajik language, although the latter is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Iranian_language" title="Western Iranian language">Western Iranian language</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing">Clothing</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Clothing">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:308px;max-width:308px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG/150px-%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG/225px-%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b0/%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG/300px-%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B5%D1%86_1.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG/150px-%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG/225px-%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG/300px-%D0%A1%D0%BE%D0%B3%D0%B4%D0%B8%D0%B9%D0%BA%D0%B0.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: a male mannequin showing the medieval-era clothing for Sogdian men from <a href="/wiki/Panjakent" title="Panjakent">Panjakent</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan_National_Museum" title="Tajikistan National Museum">Tajikistan National Museum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dushanbe" title="Dushanbe">Dushanbe</a><br/> <b>Right image</b>: a female mannequin showing the medieval-era clothing for Sogdian women from <a href="/wiki/Afrasiyab_(Samarkand)" title="Afrasiyab (Samarkand)">Afrasiyab (Samarkand)</a>, Tajikistan National Museum, Dushanbe</div></div></div>\n<p>Early medieval Sogdian costumes can be divided in two periods: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalite" title="Hephthalite">Hephtalitic</a> (5th and 6th centuries) and Turkic (7th and early 8th centuries). The latter did not become common immediately after the political dominance of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kturks" title="G&#246;kturks">G&#246;kturks</a> but only in c. 620 when, especially following <a href="/wiki/Western_Turkic_Khaganate" title="Western Turkic Khaganate">Western Turkic</a> Khagan <a href="/wiki/Tong_Yabghu_Qaghan" title="Tong Yabghu Qaghan">Ton-jazbgu</a>\'s reforms, Sogd was Turkized and the local nobility was officially included in the Khaganate\'s administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YatsenkoSogdianCostume_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-YatsenkoSogdianCostume-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>For both sexes clothes were tight-fitted, and narrow waists and wrists were appreciated. The silhouettes for grown men and young girls emphasized wide shoulders and narrowed to the waist; the silhouettes for female aristocrats were more complicated. The Sogdian clothing underwent a thorough process of Islamization in the ensuing centuries, with few of the original elements remaining. In their stead, turbans, <a href="/wiki/Kaftan" title="Kaftan">kaftans</a>, and sleeved coats became more common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-YatsenkoSogdianCostume_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-YatsenkoSogdianCostume-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_beliefs">Religious beliefs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religious beliefs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">Silk Road transmission of Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_Buddhism" title="History of Buddhism">History of Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iranian_religions" title="Iranian religions">Iranian religions</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_mythology" title="Persian mythology">Persian mythology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mar_Ammo" title="Mar Ammo">Mar Ammo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_China#Zoroastrianism" title="Religion in China">Religion in China &#167;&#160;Zoroastrianism</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sogdian_New_Year_Festival,_Northern_Qi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1122" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sogdian_New_Year_Festival%2C_Northern_Qi.jpg/220px-Sogdian_New_Year_Festival%2C_Northern_Qi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sogdian_New_Year_Festival%2C_Northern_Qi.jpg/330px-Sogdian_New_Year_Festival%2C_Northern_Qi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Sogdian_New_Year_Festival%2C_Northern_Qi.jpg/440px-Sogdian_New_Year_Festival%2C_Northern_Qi.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sogdian_New_Year_Festival,_Northern_Qi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sogdians, depicted on a Chinese Sogdian sarcophagus of the <a href="/wiki/Northern_Qi" title="Northern Qi">Northern Qi Dynasty</a> (550&#8211;577 AD)</div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:318px;max-width:318px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:152px;max-width:152px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dinastia_tang,_shanxi,_straniero_dal_volto_velato,_600-750_ca.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="1888" data-file-width="1336" decoding="async" height="212" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dinastia_tang%2C_shanxi%2C_straniero_dal_volto_velato%2C_600-750_ca.JPG/150px-Dinastia_tang%2C_shanxi%2C_straniero_dal_volto_velato%2C_600-750_ca.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dinastia_tang%2C_shanxi%2C_straniero_dal_volto_velato%2C_600-750_ca.JPG/225px-Dinastia_tang%2C_shanxi%2C_straniero_dal_volto_velato%2C_600-750_ca.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Dinastia_tang%2C_shanxi%2C_straniero_dal_volto_velato%2C_600-750_ca.JPG/300px-Dinastia_tang%2C_shanxi%2C_straniero_dal_volto_velato%2C_600-750_ca.JPG 2x" width="150"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:162px;max-width:162px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG"><img alt="" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="213" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG/160px-Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG/240px-Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG/320px-Dynastie_Tang_Barbares.JPG 2x" width="160"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent"><b>Left image</b>: An 8th-century <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> <a href="/wiki/Chinese_ceramics" title="Chinese ceramics">Chinese clay figurine</a> of a Sogdian man wearing a distinctive cap and face veil, possibly a camel rider or even a <a href="/wiki/Mobad" title="Mobad">Zoroastrian priest</a> engaging in a ritual at a <a href="/wiki/Fire_temple" title="Fire temple">fire temple</a>, since face veils were used to avoid contaminating the holy fire with breath or saliva; <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Oriental_Art_(Turin)" title="Museum of Oriental Art (Turin)">Museum of Oriental Art (Turin)</a>, Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup><br/> <b>Right image</b>: Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Dynasty</a> era statues of Sogdian merchants</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:317px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sogdians_from_T%CA%BBung-wan_City.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="299" data-file-width="450" decoding="async" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Sogdians_from_T%CA%BBung-wan_City.jpg/315px-Sogdians_from_T%CA%BBung-wan_City.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/5b/Sogdians_from_T%CA%BBung-wan_City.jpg 1.5x" width="315"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sogdians_from_T%CA%BBung-wan_City.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sogdians in a religious procession, a 5th&#8211;6th-century tomb mural discovered at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tung-wan_City" title="Tung-wan City">Tung-wan City</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The Sogdians practiced a variety of religious faiths. However, Zoroastrianism was most likely their main religion as demonstrated by material evidence. For instance, the discovery of murals depicting votaries making offers before fire-holders and <a href="/wiki/Ossuary" title="Ossuary">ossuaries</a> from Samarkand, Panjakent and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Er-Kurgan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Er-Kurgan (page does not exist)">Er-Kurgan</a> held the bones of the dead in accordance with Zoroastrian ritual. At <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a>, Sogdian burials shared similar features with traditional Chinese practices, yet they still retained essential Zoroastrian rituals, such as <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Silence" title="Tower of Silence">allowing the bodies to be picked clean</a> by <a href="/wiki/Scavenger" title="Scavenger">scavengers</a> before burying the bones in ossuaries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-5"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup> They also <a href="/wiki/Animal_sacrifice" title="Animal sacrifice">sacrificed animals</a> to Zoroastrian deities, including the supreme deity <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">Ahura Mazda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-6"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup> Zoroastrianism remained the dominant religion among Sogdians until after the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim_conquests" title="Muslim conquests">Islamic conquest</a>, when they gradually converted to Islam, as is shown by Richard Bulliet\'s "conversion curve".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tobin_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tobin-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sogdian religious texts found in China and dating to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southern_and_Northern_Dynasties#The_Northern_Dynasties" title="Southern and Northern Dynasties">Northern Dynasties</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sui_Dynasty" title="Sui Dynasty">Sui</a>, and Tang are mostly Buddhist (translated from Chinese sources), Manichaean and <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christian</a>, with only a small minority of Zoroastrian texts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grenet_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grenet-113">[113]</a></sup> But tombs of Sogdian merchants in China dated to the last third of the 6th century show predominantly Zoroastrian motifs or Zoroastrian-Manichaean syncretism, while archaeological remains from Sogdiana appear fairly Iranian and conservatively Zoroastrian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grenet_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-Grenet-113">[113]</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, the Sogdians epitomized the religious plurality found along the trade routes. The largest body of Sogdian texts are Buddhist, and Sogdians were among the principal translators of Buddhist sutras into Chinese. However, Buddhism did not take root in Sogdiana itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Additionally, the Bulayiq monastery to the north of Turpan contained Sogdian Christian texts and there are numerous Manichaean texts in Sogdiana from nearby Qocho.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup> The reconversion of Sogdians from Buddhism to Zoroastrianism coincided with the adoption of Zoroastrianism by the Sassanid Empire of Persia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2001_p168_66-2"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2001_p168-66">[66]</a></sup> From the 4th century onwards, Sogdian Buddhist pilgrims left behind evidence of their travels along the steep cliffs of the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hunza_Valley" title="Hunza Valley">Hunza Valley</a>. It was here that they carved images of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> and holy <a href="/wiki/Stupa" title="Stupa">stupas</a> in addition to their full names, in hopes that the Buddha would grant them his protection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[116]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Sogdians also practiced the faith of <a href="/wiki/Mani_(prophet)" title="Mani (prophet)">Mani</a>, Manichaeism, a faith that they spread to the Uyghurs. The <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_Khaganate" title="Uyghur Khaganate">Uyghur Khaganate</a> (744&#8211;840 AD) developed close ties to Tang China once they aided the Tang in suppressing the rebellion of An Lushan and his G&#246;kt&#252;rk successor <a href="/wiki/Shi_Siming" title="Shi Siming">Shi Siming</a>, establishing an annual trade relationship of one million bolts of Chinese silk for one hundred thousand horses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2001_p169_60-1"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2001_p169-60">[60]</a></sup> The Uyghurs relied on Sogdian merchants to sell much of this silk further west along the Silk Road, a symbiotic relationship that led many Uyghurs to adopt <a href="/wiki/Chinese_Manichaeism" title="Chinese Manichaeism">Manichaeism from the Sogdians</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liu_2001_p169_60-2"><a href="#cite_note-liu_2001_p169-60">[60]</a></sup> However, evidence of Manichaean liturgical and canonical texts of Sogdian origin remains fragmentary and sparse compared to their corpus of Buddhist writings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> The Uyghurs were also followers of Buddhism. For instance, they can be seen wearing silk robes in the <i>pra&#7751;idhi</i> scenes of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bezeklik_Thousand_Buddha_Caves" title="Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves">Uyghur Bezeklik Buddhist murals</a> of Xinjiang, China, particularly Scene 6 from Temple 9 showing <a href="/wiki/File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg" title="File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg">Sogdian donors to the Buddha</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gasparini_2014_pp134-163_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-gasparini_2014_pp134-163-77">[77]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In addition to <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranic cults</a>, there were five <a href="/wiki/Hindu_deities" title="Hindu deities">Hindu deities</a> known to have been worshipped in Sogdiana.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kumar_2007_p8_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-kumar_2007_p8-119">[119]</a></sup> These were <a href="/wiki/Brahma" title="Brahma">Brahma</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shiva" title="Shiva">Mahadeva</a> (Shiva), <a href="/wiki/Narayana" title="Narayana">Narayana</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaishravana" title="Vaishravana">Vaishravana</a>; the gods Brahma, Indra, and Shiva were known by their Sogdian names <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Zravan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Zravan (page does not exist)">Zravan</a>, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Adbad&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Adbad (page does not exist)">Adbad</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Veshparkar&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Veshparkar (page does not exist)">Veshparkar</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kumar_2007_p8_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-kumar_2007_p8-119">[119]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Durga" title="Durga">Durga</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Mother_goddess" title="Mother goddess">mother goddess</a> in <a href="/wiki/Shaktism" title="Shaktism">Shaktism</a>, may be represented in Sogdian art as a four-armed goddess riding atop a lion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kumar_2007_p8_119-2"><a href="#cite_note-kumar_2007_p8-119">[119]</a></sup> As seen in an 8th-century mural from Panjakent, portable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fire_altar" title="Fire altar">fire altars</a> can be "associated" with <a href="/wiki/Mahadeva_(Buddhism)" title="Mahadeva (Buddhism)">Mahadeva</a>-Veshparkar, Brahma-Zravan, and Indra-Abdab, according to Braja Bih&#257;r&#299; Kumar.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kumar_2007_p8_119-3"><a href="#cite_note-kumar_2007_p8-119">[119]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Among the Sogdian Christians known in China from inscriptions and texts were An Yena, a Christian from An country (Bukhara). Mi Jifen a Christian from Mi country (Maymurgh), Kang Zhitong, a Sogdian Christian cleric from Kang country (Samarkand), Mi Xuanqing a Sogdian Christian cleric from Mi country (Maymurgh), Mi Xuanying, a Sogdian Christian cleric from Mi country (Maymurgh), An Qingsu, a Sogdian Christian monk from An country (Bukhara).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup>\n</p><p>When visiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuan_Dynasty" title="Yuan Dynasty">Yuan-era</a> <a href="/wiki/Zhenjiang" title="Zhenjiang">Zhenjiang</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jiangsu" title="Jiangsu">Jiangsu</a>, China during the late 13th century, the <a href="/wiki/Venice" title="Venice">Venetian</a> explorer and merchant <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> noted that <a href="/wiki/Europeans_in_Medieval_China" title="Europeans in Medieval China">a large number</a> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_church" title="Christian church">Christian churches</a> had been built there. His claim is confirmed by a Chinese text of the 14th century explaining how a Sogdian named Mar-Sargis from Samarkand founded six <a href="/wiki/Church_of_the_East_in_China" title="Church of the East in China">Nestorian Christian churches</a> there in addition to one in <a href="/wiki/Hangzhou" title="Hangzhou">Hangzhou</a> during the second half of the 13th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Nestorian Christianity had existed in China earlier during the Tang Dynasty when a Persian monk named <a href="/wiki/Alopen" title="Alopen">Alopen</a> came to Chang\'an in 653 to <a href="/wiki/Proselytism" title="Proselytism">proselytize</a>, as described in a dual Chinese and <a href="/wiki/Syriac_language" title="Syriac language">Syriac language</a> inscription from Chang\'an (modern Xi\'an) dated to the year 781.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Within the Syriac inscription is a list of priests and monks, one of whom is named Gabriel, the <a href="/wiki/Archdeacon" title="Archdeacon">archdeacon</a> of "Xumdan" and "Sarag", the Sogdian names for the Chinese capital cities <a href="/wiki/Chang%27an" title="Chang\'an">Chang\'an</a> and <a href="/wiki/Luoyang" title="Luoyang">Luoyang</a>, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> In regards to textual material, the earliest Christian <a href="/wiki/Gospel" title="Gospel">gospel</a> texts <a href="/wiki/Bible_translations_into_Sogdian" title="Bible translations into Sogdian">translated into Sogdian</a> coincide with the reign of the Sasanian Persian monarch <a href="/wiki/Yazdegerd_II" title="Yazdegerd II">Yazdegerd II</a> (r. 438&#8211;457) and were translated from the <i><a href="/wiki/Peshitta" title="Peshitta">Peshitta</a></i>, the standard version of the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a> in <a href="/wiki/Syriac_Christianity" title="Syriac Christianity">Syriac Christianity</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commerce_and_slave_trade">Commerce and slave trade</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Commerce and slave trade">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_slavery_in_China" title="History of slavery in China">History of slavery in China</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iranians_in_China" title="Iranians in China">Iranians in China</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sogdiana,_piatto_con_tigre,_argento_dorato_e_niellato,_VII-VIII_sec.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1602" data-file-width="1618" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sogdiana%2C_piatto_con_tigre%2C_argento_dorato_e_niellato%2C_VII-VIII_sec.JPG/220px-Sogdiana%2C_piatto_con_tigre%2C_argento_dorato_e_niellato%2C_VII-VIII_sec.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sogdiana%2C_piatto_con_tigre%2C_argento_dorato_e_niellato%2C_VII-VIII_sec.JPG/330px-Sogdiana%2C_piatto_con_tigre%2C_argento_dorato_e_niellato%2C_VII-VIII_sec.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Sogdiana%2C_piatto_con_tigre%2C_argento_dorato_e_niellato%2C_VII-VIII_sec.JPG/440px-Sogdiana%2C_piatto_con_tigre%2C_argento_dorato_e_niellato%2C_VII-VIII_sec.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sogdiana,_piatto_con_tigre,_argento_dorato_e_niellato,_VII-VIII_sec.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Sogdian <a href="/wiki/Gilding" title="Gilding">gilded</a> <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> dish with the image of a <a href="/wiki/Tiger" title="Tiger">tiger</a>, with clear influence from Persian <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_art" title="Sasanian art">Sasanian art</a> and silverwares, 7th to 8th centuries AD</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2988" data-file-width="5312" decoding="async" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg/220px-Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg/330px-Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg/440px-Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silk_road_figure_head_probably_Sogdian.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silk road figure head, probably Sogdian, Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sui_Dynasty" title="Sui Dynasty">Sui Dynasty</a> (581&#8211;618), <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_Cernuschi" title="Mus&#233;e Cernuschi">Mus&#233;e Cernuschi</a>, Paris</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">Slavery</a> existed in China since ancient times, although during the Han dynasty the proportion of slaves to the overall population was roughly 1%,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> far lower than the estimate for the contemporary <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_world" title="Greco-Roman world">Greco-Roman world</a> (estimated at about 15% of <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Demography of the Roman Empire">the entire population</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> During the Tang period slaves were not allowed to marry a commoner\'s daughter, were not allowed to have sexual relations with any female member of their master\'s family, and although fornication with female slaves was forbidden in the <a href="/wiki/Tang_Code" title="Tang Code">Tang code of law</a> it was widely practiced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Manumission" title="Manumission">Manumission</a> was also permitted when a slave woman gave birth to her master\'s son, which allowed for her elevation to the legal status of a commoner, yet she could only live as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Concubine" title="Concubine">concubine</a> and not as the wife of her former master.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sogdian and Chinese merchants regularly traded in slaves in and around Turpan during the Tang dynasty. In 639 a female Sogdian slave was sold to a Chinese man as recorded in an <a href="/wiki/Astana" title="Astana">Astana</a> cemetery legal document written in Sogdian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Skaff2012_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Skaff2012-132">[132]</a></sup> Khotan and <a href="/wiki/Kucha" title="Kucha">Kucha</a> were places where women were commonly sold, with ample evidence of the slave trade in Turfan thanks to contemporary textual sources that have survived.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrombertVaissi&#232;re2005_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-TrombertVaissi&#232;re2005-133">[133]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrombertVaissi&#232;re_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-TrombertVaissi&#232;re-134">[134]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tang_poetry" title="Tang poetry">Tang poetry</a> Sogdian girls also frequently appear as <a href="/wiki/Waiting_staff" title="Waiting staff">serving maids</a> in the taverns and inns of the capital Chang\'an.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[135]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Sogdian slave girls and their Chinese male owners made up the majority of Sogdian female-Chinese male pairings, while free Sogdian women were the most common spouse of Sogdian men. A smaller number of Chinese women were paired with elite Sogdian men. Sogdian man-and-woman pairings made up eighteen out of twenty-one marriages according to existing documents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrombertVaissi&#232;re_134-1"><a href="#cite_note-TrombertVaissi&#232;re-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrombertVaissi&#232;re2005_2_136-0"><a href="#cite_note-TrombertVaissi&#232;re2005_2-136">[136]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A document dated 731 AD reveals that precisely forty bolts of silk were paid to a certain Mi Lushan, a slave dealing Sogdian, by a Chinese man named Tang Rong (&#21776;&#27054;) of Chang\'an, for the purchase of an eleven-year-old girl. A person from Xizhou, a Tokharistani (i.e. Bactrian), and three Sogdians verified the sale of the girl.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrombertVaissi&#232;re_134-2"><a href="#cite_note-TrombertVaissi&#232;re-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TrombertVaissi&#232;re2005_3_137-0"><a href="#cite_note-TrombertVaissi&#232;re2005_3-137">[137]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_historiography">Modern historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Modern historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/German_Turfan_expeditions" title="German Turfan expeditions">German Turfan expeditions</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_von_Le_Coq" title="Albert von Le Coq">Albert von Le Coq</a></div>\n<p>In 1916 the French <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sinologist" title="Sinologist">Sinologist</a> and historian <a href="/wiki/Paul_Pelliot" title="Paul Pelliot">Paul Pelliot</a> used <a href="/wiki/Dunhuang_manuscripts" title="Dunhuang manuscripts">Tang Chinese manuscripts</a> excavated from Dunhuang, Gansu to identify an ancient Sogdian colony south of <a href="/wiki/Lop_Nur" title="Lop Nur">Lop Nur</a> in Xinjiang (Northwest China), which he argued was the base for the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road_transmission_of_Buddhism" title="Silk Road transmission of Buddhism">spread of Buddhism</a> and Nestorian Christianity in China.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rong_2009_p148_138-0"><a href="#cite_note-rong_2009_p148-138">[138]</a></sup> In 1926 Japanese scholar Kuwabara compiled evidence for Sogdians in Chinese historical sources and by 1933 Chinese historian Xiang Da published his <i>Tang Chang\'an and Central Asian Culture</i> detailing the Sogdian influence on Chinese social religious life in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Xi%27an" title="History of Xi\'an">Tang-era Chinese capital city</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rong_2009_p148_138-1"><a href="#cite_note-rong_2009_p148-138">[138]</a></sup> The Canadian Sinologist <a href="/wiki/Edwin_G._Pulleyblank" title="Edwin G. Pulleyblank">Edwin G. Pulleyblank</a> published an article in 1952 demonstrating the presence of a Sogdian colony founded in Six Hu Prefectures of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ordos_Loop" title="Ordos Loop">Ordos Loop</a> during the Chinese Tang period, composed of Sogdians and Turkic peoples who migrated from the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian-Manchurian_grassland" title="Mongolian-Manchurian grassland">Mongolian steppe</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-rong_2009_p148_138-2"><a href="#cite_note-rong_2009_p148-138">[138]</a></sup> The Japanese historian Ikeda On wrote an article in 1965 outlining the history of the Sogdians inhabiting Dunhuang from the beginning of the 7th century, analyzing lists of their <a href="/wiki/Chinese_surname" title="Chinese surname">Sinicized names</a> and the role of Zoroastrianism and Buddhism in their religious life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup> Yoshida Yutaka and Kageyama Etsuko, Japanese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnographer" title="Ethnographer">ethnographers</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linguist" title="Linguist">linguists</a> of the Sogdian language, were able to reconstruct Sogdian names from forty-five different Chinese <a href="/wiki/Transliteration" title="Transliteration">transliterations</a>, noting that these were common in Turfan whereas Sogdians living closer to the center of Chinese civilization for generations adopted traditional <a href="/wiki/Chinese_name" title="Chinese name">Chinese names</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-7"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_Sogdians">Notable Sogdians</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sogdia&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Notable Sogdians">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KhunakCoin.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="365" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/KhunakCoin.jpg/220px-KhunakCoin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/KhunakCoin.jpg/330px-KhunakCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/KhunakCoin.jpg/440px-KhunakCoin.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:KhunakCoin.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A minted <a href="/wiki/Bukhar_Khudahs" title="Bukhar Khudahs">coin of Khunak</a>, king of <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>, early 8th century, showing the <a href="/wiki/Crown_(headgear)" title="Crown (headgear)">crowned</a> king <a href="/wiki/Obverse_and_reverse" title="Obverse and reverse">on the obverse</a>, and a <a href="/wiki/Fire_temple" title="Fire temple">Zoroastrian fire altar</a> on the reverse</div></div></div>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:490px;max-width:490px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Yaghnobi_boy.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="535" data-file-width="518" decoding="async" height="196" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Yaghnobi_boy.jpg/190px-Yaghnobi_boy.jpg" 2x" width="190"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:294px;max-width:294px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:11Yagnobi_people.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/11Yagnobi_people.jpg/292px-11Yagnobi_people.jpg" //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/11Yagnobi_people.jpg/584px-11Yagnobi_people.jpg 2x" width="292"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Ethnic Yaghnobi children of <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Yaghnobi_people" title="Yaghnobi people">Yaghnobi people</a> speak <a href="/wiki/Yaghnobi_language" title="Yaghnobi language">a language</a> that is a direct descendant of <a href="/wiki/Sogdian_language" title="Sogdian language">medieval Sogdian</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1217_65-5"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1217-65">[65]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bezeklik_caves,_Pranidhi_scene_14,_temple_9.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="708" data-file-width="524" decoding="async" height="297" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bezeklik_caves%2C_Pranidhi_scene_14%2C_temple_9.JPG/220px-Bezeklik_caves%2C_Pranidhi_scene_14%2C_temple_9.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bezeklik_caves%2C_Pranidhi_scene_14%2C_temple_9.JPG/330px-Bezeklik_caves%2C_Pranidhi_scene_14%2C_temple_9.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Bezeklik_caves%2C_Pranidhi_scene_14%2C_temple_9.JPG/440px-Bezeklik_caves%2C_Pranidhi_scene_14%2C_temple_9.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bezeklik_caves,_Pranidhi_scene_14,_temple_9.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pranidhi scene, temple 9 (Cave 20) of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bezeklik_Thousand_Buddha_Caves" title="Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves">Bezeklik Thousand Buddha Caves</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Turfan" title="Turfan">Turfan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xinjiang" title="Xinjiang">Xinjiang</a>, China, 9th century AD, with kneeling figures praying in front of the Buddha who <a href="/wiki/Albert_von_Le_Coq" title="Albert von Le Coq">Albert von Le Coq</a> assumed were <a href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persian people</a> (German: "Perser"), noting their <a href="/wiki/Caucasian_race" title="Caucasian race">Caucasian</a> features and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green-eyed" title="Green-eyed">green eyes</a>, and comparing the hat of the man on the left (in the green coat) to <a href="/wiki/Headgear" title="Headgear">headgear</a> worn by <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_art" title="Sasanian art">Sasanian Persian princes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup> However, modern scholarship has identified <a href="/wiki/File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg" title="File:BezeklikSogdianMerchants.jpg"><i>pra&#7751;idhi</i> scenes of the same temple</a> (No. 9) as depicting Sogdians,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gasparini_2014_pp134-163_77-2"><a href="#cite_note-gasparini_2014_pp134-163-77">[77]</a></sup> who inhabited Turfan as an ethnic minority during the phases of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tang_Dynasty" title="Tang Dynasty">Tang Chinese</a> (7th&#8211;8th century) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Qocho" title="Kingdom of Qocho">Uyghur rule</a> (9th&#8211;13th century).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hansen_2012_p98_78-8"><a href="#cite_note-hansen_2012_p98-78">[78]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Alexander_IV_of_Macedon" title="Alexander IV of Macedon">Alexander IV of Macedon</a>, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilius" title="Basilius">Basilius</a></i> of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macedon" title="Macedon">Macedon</a>, son of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander the Great</a> and <a href="/wiki/Roxana" title="Roxana">Roxana</a>, the latter a daughter of the Sogdian nobleman <a href="/wiki/Oxyartes" title="Oxyartes">Oxyartes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahmed_2004_p61_27-2"><a href="#cite_note-ahmed_2004_p61-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-livius_roxane_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-livius_roxane-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-strachan_2008_p87_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-strachan_2008_p87-29">[29]</a></sup> making Alexander IV half-Sogdian</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a> (&#23433;&#31103;&#23665;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p135_79-4"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p135-79">[79]</a></sup> a military leader of Sogdian (from his father\'s side) and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%B6kturks" title="G&#246;kturks">T&#363;ju&#233;</a> origin during the <a href="/wiki/Tang_dynasty" title="Tang dynasty">Tang dynasty</a> in China; he rose to prominence by fighting (and losing) frontier wars between 741 and 755. Later, he precipitated the catastrophic <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan_Rebellion" title="An Lushan Rebellion">An Lushan Rebellion</a>, which lasted from 755 to 763 and led to the decline of the Tang dynasty.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/An_Qingxu" title="An Qingxu">An Qingxu</a> (&#23433;&#24950;&#32210;), son of <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/An_Chonghui" title="An Chonghui">An Chonghui</a> (&#23433;&#37325;&#35496;), a minister of China\'s <a href="/wiki/Later_Tang" title="Later Tang">Later Tang</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/An_Congjin" title="An Congjin">An Congjin</a> (&#23433;&#24478;&#36914;), a general of Later Tang and China\'s <a href="/wiki/Later_Jin_(Five_Dynasties)" title="Later Jin (Five Dynasties)">Later Jin (Five Dynasties)</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/An_Chongrong" title="An Chongrong">An Chongrong</a> (&#23433;&#37325;&#27054;), a general of the China\'s Later Jin (Five Dynasties)</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Antiochus_I_Soter" title="Antiochus I Soter">Antiochus I Soter</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magill_et_al_1998_p1010_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-magill_et_al_1998_p1010-35">[35]</a></sup> second king of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a>, who was half-Sogdian and Macedonian-Greek due to his maternal (<a href="/wiki/Apama" title="Apama">Apama</a>) and paternal (<a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a>) lineage, respectively</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Apama" title="Apama">Apama</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-magill_et_al_1998_p1010_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-magill_et_al_1998_p1010-35">[35]</a></sup> daughter of <a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a> (see below) and wife of <a href="/wiki/Seleucus_I_Nicator" title="Seleucus I Nicator">Seleucus I Nicator</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/Seleucid_Empire" title="Seleucid Empire">Seleucid Empire</a></li>\n<li><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Azanes_(general)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Azanes (general) (page does not exist)">Azanes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dresden_2003_p1216_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-dresden_2003_p1216-7">[7]</a></sup> son of Artaios, who led a contingent of Sogdian troops in the <a href="/wiki/Military_history_of_Iran" title="Military history of Iran">Persian army</a> of <a href="/wiki/Xerxes_I" title="Xerxes I">Xerxes I</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Second_Persian_invasion_of_Greece" title="Second Persian invasion of Greece">Second Persian invasion of Greece</a> in 480 BC</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Divashtich" title="Divashtich">Divashtich</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> 8th-century ruler of <a href="/wiki/Panjakent" title="Panjakent">Panjakent</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Fazang" title="Fazang">Fazang</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gernet1996_2_142-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gernet1996_2-142">[142]</a></sup> Buddhist monk of the 7th century, a colleague of <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Gurak" title="Gurak">Gurak</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-litvinski_et_al_1999_p459_91-2"><a href="#cite_note-litvinski_et_al_1999_p459-91">[91]</a></sup> 8th-century ruler of <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Kang_Senghui" title="Kang Senghui">Kang Senghui</a> (&#24247;&#20711;&#26371;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nguyen2008_143-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nguyen2008-143">[143]</a></sup> Buddhist monk of the 3rd century who lived in <a href="/wiki/Jiaozhi" title="Jiaozhi">Jiaozhi</a> (modern-day <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>) during the <a href="/wiki/Three_Kingdoms" title="Three Kingdoms">Three Kingdoms</a> period</li>\n<li>Kang Jing (&#24247;&#26223;)?- a possible Sogdian who worked at the <a href="/wiki/Ming_dynasty" title="Ming dynasty">Ming dynasty</a> Mansion of the Prince of Qin (<a class="extiw" href="https://zh.wikipedia.org/wiki/%E6%98%8E%E6%9C%9D%E8%97%A9%E7%8E%8B%E5%88%97%E8%A1%A8_(%E7%A7%A6%E7%8E%8B%E7%B3%BB)" title="zh:&#26126;&#26397;&#34281;&#29579;&#21015;&#34920; (&#31206;&#29579;&#31995;)">&#26126;&#26397;&#34281;&#29579;&#21015;&#34920; (&#31206;&#29579;&#31995;)</a>) as a servant<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a Kawus al-Afshin</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raffat&#699;Alav&#299;1985_146-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raffat&#699;Alav&#299;1985-146">[146]</a></sup> a general of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abbasid_caliphate" title="Abbasid caliphate">Abbasid caliphate</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Vassal" title="Vassal">vassal</a> of the Abbasids as the prince of <a href="/wiki/Osrushana" title="Osrushana">Osrushana</a> during the 9th century</li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kaydar_Nasr_ibn_%27Abdallah" title="Kaydar Nasr ibn \'Abdallah">Kaydar Nasr ibn \'Abdallah</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> Abbasid governor of <a href="/wiki/Egypt_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Egypt in the Middle Ages">Egypt</a> during the 9th century</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Li_Baoyu" title="Li Baoyu">Li Baoyu</a> (&#26446;&#25265;&#29577;),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-howard_2012_p135_79-5"><a href="#cite_note-howard_2012_p135-79">[79]</a></sup> formerly known as An Chongzhang (&#23433;&#37325;&#29835;) and <a href="/wiki/Chinese_nobility" title="Chinese nobility">ennobled</a> as Duke Zhaowu of <a href="/wiki/Western_Liang_(Sixteen_Kingdoms)" title="Western Liang (Sixteen Kingdoms)">Liang</a> (&#28092;&#26157;&#27494;&#20844;), a general of the Chinese Tang Dynasty who fought against the rebellion of <a href="/wiki/An_Lushan" title="An Lushan">An Lushan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Tibetan_Empire" title="Tibetan Empire">Tibetan Empire</a></li>\n<li><a caliphate.</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Muzaffar_ibn_Kaydar" title="Muzaffar ibn Kaydar">Muzaffar ibn Kaydar</a>, son of Kaydar Nasr ibn \'Abdallah (see above), and yet another Abbasid governor of Egypt during the 9th century</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Oxyartes" title="Oxyartes">Oxyartes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ahmed_2004_p61_27-3"><a id="cite_ref-strachan_2008_p87_29-2"><a href="#cite_note-strachan_2008_p87-29">[29]</a></sup> Sogdian warlord from <a href="/wiki/Bactria" title="Bactria">Bactria</a>, follower of <a href="#cite_note-strachan_2008_p87-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> the primary wife of Alexander the Great during the 4th century BC</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Spitamenes" title="Spitamenes">Spitamenes</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-holt_1989_pp64-65_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-holt_1989_pp64-65-32">[32]</a></sup> a Sogdian warlord who led an uprising against Alexander the Great in the late 4th century BC</li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tarkhun" title="Tarkhun">Tarkhun</a>,<sup class="reference" class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diaspora_areas">Diaspora areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span
